10107-8{_}1044{_}A few Austrians reached our parapet, but none got into our trenches.
10107-8{_}1045{_}Firing died down about a quarter to seven, and the Brigadier came up to the O.P., very pleased with the support we had rendered, and asked that a slow rate of fire might be kept up.
10107-8{_}1046{_}Later on an Austrian telephone message was overheard, which suggested that the attack was to be renewed just before dawn, after a gas attack.
10107-8{_}1047{_}We kept on the alert, but nothing happened.
10107-8{_}1048{_}Two of our Batteries went on firing at a slow rate all night.
10107-8{_}1049{_}When dawn broke, it was evident that our bombardment had been very destructive.
10107-8{_}1050{_}The enemy's trenches were knocked to pieces; uprooted trees, planks, sandbags and dead bodies lay about in confusion.
10107-8{_}1051{_}It was thought that owing to our fire some Austrian units, which were to have taken part in the attack, could not, and others would not, do so, in spite of a special issue of rum and other spirits.
10107-8{_}1052{_}I saw also, motionless amid the Austrian wire, a figure in Italian uniform, one of a patrol who had gone out four nights before, and had not returned.
10107-8{_}1053{_}On the 12th I went out with a Sergeant, a Signaller and Corporal Savogna, a Canadian Italian, on a Front Line Reconnaissance on the northern side of the Vippacco, in the Second Army area.
10107-8{_}1054{_}The day was wonderfully clear and we could see the everlasting snows beyond Cadore.
10107-8{_}1055{_}We went through Rupa to Merna and, being evidently spotted, were shelled with 4.2's and forced to proceed along a muddy communication trench knee deep in water.
10107-8{_}1056{_}At Raccogliano Mill we visited the Headquarters of the Bergamo Brigade, which was holding the line.
10107-8{_}1057{_}A guide took us along the front line, which had been considerably advanced here in August and September, and again by a successful local attack a few days before.
10107-8{_}1058{_}We went down one _Caverna_ in which, on the occasion of this last attack, a Magyar officer and 25 men surrendered.
10107-8{_}1059{_}The Austrian sentry, also a Magyar, had been fastened by the leg to the doorpost outside the entrance to the dug-out.
10107-8{_}1060{_}In the Italian bombardment one of his feet was blown away, but his own people had done nothing for him.
10107-8{_}1061{_}Now his dead body lay out in the open behind the new Italian front line.
10107-8{_}1062{_}*       *       *       *       *  On the 14th Jeune went on leave to England, no one having any expectation that anything of importance was likely to happen in the near future.
10107-8{_}1063{_}In his absence I acted as Second-in-Command of the Battery.
10107-8{_}1064{_}On the 19th we heard that the Italian High Command was preparing another big offensive from the Bainsizza against the Ternova Plateau, and the same day the Intelligence Report contained the information that a series of German Divisions had been seen detraining at Lubiana since the beginning of October, and that, owing to the Russian collapse, a thousand Austrian guns had been moved across from the Russian to the Isonzo Front since the middle of September.
10107-8{_}1065{_}We had noticed a perceptible increase in the enemy's Artillery activity for some time, but this, we thought at the time, was purely defensive.
10107-8{_}1066{_}There had also been a week of heavy rains, but the Vippacco, after rising rapidly and threatening to flood us all out, fell eighteen inches in one night.
10107-8{_}1067{_}It swept away a number of Italian bridges, however, from Merna and Raccogliano further up stream, and we saw pieces of these rushing past in the swift current.
10107-8{_}1068{_}On the 21st the Major and I motored to Palmanova and bought some winter clothing at the Ordnance.
10107-8{_}1069{_}An Austrian twelve-inch howitzer, whom we had christened "Mr Pongo," was shelling all day at intervals, chiefly in the back areas.
10107-8{_}1070{_}An unpleasant beast, we agreed, who wanted smothering!
10107-8{_}1071{_}On the 22nd it was evident, from the Austrian shelling, that quite a number of fresh heavy howitzers, both twelve- and fifteen-inch, had appeared behind the Austrian lines.
10107-8{_}1072{_}A few, no doubt, of those thousand guns from Russia!
10107-8{_}1073{_}Listening to their shells whistling over one's head like express trains, and to their (happily distant) deep crashes on percussion, one realised very vividly the immediate military effects of the Russian collapse.
10107-8{_}1074{_}We heard that the Italian offensive was not coming off after all.
10107-8{_}1075{_}On the 23rd we heard that a big Austrian attack was expected last night and might come that night instead.
10107-8{_}1076{_}We received orders to clean up and prepare, in case of necessity, the old position at Boschini on San Michele, which the Battery had occupied when they first arrived in Italy.
10107-8{_}1077{_}This, I thought, seemed rather panic-stricken.
10107-8{_}1078{_}Romano's Battery had similar orders.
10107-8{_}1079{_}It would be annoying to leave our present position after all the work put into it to make it habitable for the winter.
10107-8{_}1080{_}But I noted that the atmosphere was tinged with apprehension.
10107-8{_}1081{_}PART IV  THE ITALIAN RETREAT AND RECOVERY   CHAPTER XX  THE BEGINNING OF THE ENEMY OFFENSIVE  On the morning of October 24th soon after nine o'clock the enemy launched a big attack against the Third Army Front, especially violent between Faiti and the Vippacco, and renewed it in the afternoon.
10107-8{_}1082{_}But he gained no ground.
10107-8{_}1083{_}All through the previous night and all that day till evening the bombardment on both sides was heavy.
10107-8{_}1084{_}We had not fired during the night but began at seven in the morning and went on throughout the day.
10107-8{_}1085{_}A message came in that the enemy would probably shell Batteries for four hours with gas shell, starting with irritant gas and going on to poison.
10107-8{_}1086{_}He had already employed these tactics up north, as we learned later.
10107-8{_}1087{_}Gas alert was on all night and we were listening strainedly for soft bursts.
10107-8{_}1088{_}Heavy rain came down steadily all day, and everything was drenched and dripping.
10107-8{_}1089{_}The spaces between our huts filled with water, and needed continual baling out.
10107-8{_}1090{_}But when gas was expected, one welcomed heavy rain[1] and high winds and loud explosions from bursting shells.
10107-8{_}1091{_}[Footnote 1: It was not till a later date that gases were employed, the effects of which were increased by rain.]
10107-8{_}1092{_}Between nine and ten p.m.
10107-8{_}1093{_}I heard a series of soft bursts just across the river and arranged with Romano's Battery for mutual alarms if any gas should come too near.
10107-8{_}1094{_}An hour later I was relieved in the Command Post and turned in.
10107-8{_}1095{_}As I was undressing, I heard the wind rising again and the telephonists next door baling out their dug-out.
10107-8{_}1096{_}We were keeping up a desultory fire all night to harass any further attacks that might be attempted.
10107-8{_}1097{_}The Major, who had been out on a Front Line Reconnaissance that morning in the neighbourhood of Merna, had come in for some very heavy shelling and returned very weary.
10107-8{_}1098{_}*       *       *       *       *  The next day, the 25th, was at least fine; it was even rather sunny.
10107-8{_}1099{_}We did a little firing, but not much, between seven a.m. and two p.m. Enemy planes came over continually, flying very low, about thirty in the course of the morning.
10107-8{_}1100{_}They attacked one of our observation balloons, which descended rapidly as they approached, and I think got down safely.
10107-8{_}1101{_}Italian anti-aircraft guns brought down one of them.
10107-8{_}1102{_}Whenever we shelled Mandria, a little village up the valley, a plane came over.
10107-8{_}1103{_}Evidently they had something there as to which they were sensitive, perhaps a General's Billet!
10107-8{_}1104{_}At half past ten the Italians ditched a lorry full of ammunition just at the top of the road from the Battery position to Pec village, in full view of the enemy on Hill 464.
10107-8{_}1105{_}At this time the village was being heavily shelled by 5.9's, and our cookhouse on the outskirts was all but hit, shells bursting all round it in a circle.
10107-8{_}1106{_}Showers of bricks and lumps of earth and masonry rose high in the air.
10107-8{_}1107{_}One shell hit the Artillery Group Headquarters of Major Borghese and I saw all his office papers going up, a cloud of shreds, shining in the sun.
10107-8{_}1108{_}I laughed and said to myself, "There goes a lot of red tape!"
10107-8{_}1109{_}I saw Borghese himself later in the day limping along with a stick; a chunk of one of his office walls had fallen on his foot.
10107-8{_}1110{_}The enemy meanwhile had begun to shell the lorry, methodically as their idiotic habit was, with one shell every five minutes.
10107-8{_}1111{_}It was too near us to be pleasant, so the Major took out a party and hauled it out of their view under cover of a bank.
10107-8{_}1112{_}But this took some time.
10107-8{_}1113{_}Leary stood by with a stopwatch calling out the minutes.
10107-8{_}1114{_}At the end of every fourth minute, the party ran for cover.
10107-8{_}1115{_}Then a few seconds later we heard the next shell coming.
10107-8{_}1116{_}The Major was hit on the hand once by a shell splinter which drew blood, but nothing more serious than this happened.
10107-8{_}1117{_}About two o'clock a big bombardment worked up again, and the Volconiac and Faiti became a sea of smoke and flame.
10107-8{_}1118{_}This went on till dusk, we firing hard all the time.
10107-8{_}1119{_}More enemy planes came over, one even after dark, a most unusual thing, flying very low indeed, under a heavy fire of anti-aircraft Batteries and machine guns from the ground.
10107-8{_}1120{_}Our planes had been very scarce all day.
10107-8{_}1121{_}They had nearly all gone north.
10107-8{_}1122{_}For the time being we had quite lost the command of the air in this sector.
10107-8{_}1123{_}The two British Batteries who were furthest forward had orders to move back that night to reserve positions on San Michele.
10107-8{_}1124{_}The Italians were going to horse their guns, for it was said that the majority of the tractors had gone north too.
10107-8{_}1125{_}This move looked rather panicky, I thought.
10107-8{_}1126{_}Many red rockets went up in the early evening from Volconiac and Faiti.
10107-8{_}1127{_}The enemy were making another attack.
10107-8{_}1128{_}Then a little later tricolour rockets, red, white and green, went up.
10107-8{_}1129{_}This was the signal that the attack had been beaten off and that the situation was quiet again.
10107-8{_}1130{_}The firing died down about seven.
10107-8{_}1131{_}We fed and put up for the night an Italian officer, whose Battery used to be here, but had moved north yesterday.
10107-8{_}1132{_}He had just come back from a gas course at Palmanova.
10107-8{_}1133{_}From a newspaper which he had I saw that a strong offensive had begun on the afternoon of the 23rd to the north of the Bainsizza Plateau.
10107-8{_}1134{_}Either the attacks here were only holding attacks, or the attack to the north was a feint and the real thing was to be here.
10107-8{_}1135{_}Anyhow, I thought, it is their Last Despairing Great Cry!
10107-8{_}1136{_}I turned in just after midnight.
10107-8{_}1137{_}The night was still and there was a bright moon and stars.
10107-8{_}1138{_}A thick mist lay along the Vippacco, just behind the trees.
10107-8{_}1139{_}The air was damp and cold.
10107-8{_}1140{_}It seemed pretty quiet for the moment all along the Front.
10107-8{_}1141{_}*       *       *       *       *  I had a troubled night.
10107-8{_}1142{_}In the early morning we were bombarded with gas shell and had to wear respirators from a quarter to three till four o'clock.
10107-8{_}1143{_}We were firing from five till six and again steadily from a quarter past seven onwards.
10107-8{_}1144{_}We got orders to move back that night to Boschini, on San Michele.
10107-8{_}1145{_}I thought this a great mistake.
10107-8{_}1146{_}Later in the day our move was cancelled, as the two forward Batteries which pulled out last night would not be in action on San Michele till to-morrow.
10107-8{_}1147{_}They had been last heard of stuck fast in a crush of traffic at the bottom of the hill at Peteano.
10107-8{_}1148{_}A strong team of horses were straining their guts out in vain attempts to pull an Italian twelve-inch mortar up the hill.
10107-8{_}1149{_}It was this which had caused the block.
10107-8{_}1150{_}Those two forward Batteries _might_ have lost their guns in a quick retreat, I thought, but hardly we.
10107-8{_}1151{_}It seemed to be feared, however, that the two bridges across the Vippacco might go.
10107-8{_}1152{_}That day we were shelled heavily with every kind of weapon, from fifteen-inch downwards, especially the Left Section in the afternoon.
10107-8{_}1153{_}We had, as usual, marvellously good luck, and only had one casualty, and that a slight wound.
10107-8{_}1154{_}The spirit and endurance of the men were wonderful.
10107-8{_}1155{_}Enemy planes were over all day; we counted twenty-two between daybreak and four p.m.
10107-8{_}1156{_}Some hovered overhead and ranged their guns on us.
10107-8{_}1157{_}Several times we put our detachments under cover and ceased fire owing to the shelling.
10107-8{_}1158{_}My own gun was half buried by a great shower of earth kicked up by a 9.45, which pitched right on top of the bank in front of us.
10107-8{_}1159{_}But Cotes, my Sergeant, and myself, crouching under cover of the girdles, were quite unhurt.
10107-8{_}1160{_}The rest of the detachment had been ordered down into their dug-out.
10107-8{_}1161{_}Another time the enemy neatly bracketed our Command Post with twelve-inch, and several of us within were uncomfortably awaiting the next round.
10107-8{_}1162{_}But luckily for us he switched away to the right.
10107-8{_}1163{_}We had to fire hard most of the day, especially in the afternoon and evening.
10107-8{_}1164{_}It had been exhausting and almost sleepless work for the detachments for several days past, for Darrell and a working party of forty were away preparing the reserve position on San Michele, and we had hardly any reliefs for the guns.
10107-8{_}1165{_}The Major, too, looked very tired and frayed, but, whenever our eyes met, he gave me a smile of encouragement and leadership.
10107-8{_}1166{_}That evening, during a short break in the firing, he asked me, since he himself could not leave the Command Post, to go round and "buck the men up" and thank them on his behalf for the way in which they had behaved.
10107-8{_}1167{_}"So long as the Major's pleased, we're satisfied," said one man.
10107-8{_}1168{_}Another, a Bombardier who afterwards got a Commission, and had been with Darrell on a reconnaissance on Faiti a few days before and had nearly been killed on the journey, said, "Well, Sir, we were thinking of the boys in the Front Line today."
10107-8{_}1169{_}And well he might, for it had been a hellish bombardment up there.
10107-8{_}1170{_}After delivering my message to the men, I walked up and down the road in front of the guns for a few moments in the short silence, realising how the Alliance of Britain and Italy was burning itself more deeply than ever into our hearts in these days of trial.
10107-8{_}1171{_}That night the enemy attacked again, and we lost Faiti and Hill 393, and had to fire on them.
10107-8{_}1172{_}I heard afterwards from the Group that Colonel Canale, when he gave the order to fire on 393, was almost weeping on the telephone.
10107-8{_}1173{_}Next day we counter-attacked and retook Faiti, but 393 remained in Austrian hands.
10107-8{_}1174{_}Rumours and denials of rumours came in from the north.
10107-8{_}1175{_}It was said that we had lost Monte Nero and Caporetto, and that German Batteries had kept up a high concentration of gas for four hours on our lines in the Cadore.
10107-8{_}1176{_}And we knew that the Italian gas masks were only guaranteed to last for an hour and a half in such conditions, and that each man only carried one.
10107-8{_}1177{_}CHAPTER XXI  FROM THE VIPPACCO TO SAN GIORGIO DI NOGARA  On the 27th the rumours became bad.
10107-8{_}1178{_}The German advance to the north was said to be considerable and rapid.
10107-8{_}1179{_}Orders came that all the British Batteries were to pull out and park that night at Villa Viola, behind Gradisca, "for duty on another part of the Front."
10107-8{_}1180{_}Probably, we thought, we were going north.
10107-8{_}1181{_}"The gun concentration up there must be awful," said the Major.
10107-8{_}1182{_}I told Cotes that we were probably going into the thick of it, and his eyes shone with pride.
10107-8{_}1183{_}He was a fine fellow.
10107-8{_}1184{_}That day the sun was shining, and the Italian planes in this sector seemed to have regained command of the air.
10107-8{_}1185{_}For the moment there was a little lull in the firing, but we felt that some big fate was looming over us.
10107-8{_}1186{_}I went away to my hut for five minutes and wrote in my diary, "I here put it on record once more that I am proud to fight in and for Italy.
10107-8{_}1187{_}I repeat that dying here is not death, it is flying into the dawn!
10107-8{_}1188{_}If I die in and for Italy, I would like to think that my death would do something for Anglo-Italian sympathy and understanding."
10107-8{_}1189{_}In the early afternoon the Major went down to Headquarters.
10107-8{_}1190{_}He rang me up from there to say that two guns were to be pulled out at once, and the other two to double their rate of fire.
10107-8{_}1191{_}No.
10107-8{_}1192{_}4 gun was now engaging two different targets with alternate rounds and different charges.
10107-8{_}1193{_}When the Major came back, he called all the men together and said.
10107-8{_}1194{_}"I am not going to conceal anything from you.
10107-8{_}1195{_}The situation is serious.
10107-8{_}1196{_}The Italians have had a bad reverse up north.
10107-8{_}1197{_}But there is no need for anyone to get panicky.
10107-8{_}1198{_}We shall pull out and go back to-night.
10107-8{_}1199{_}That is all I know at present.
10107-8{_}1200{_}When I know more, I will tell you more.
10107-8{_}1201{_}One gun will remain in action till the last.
10107-8{_}1202{_}No.
10107-8{_}1203{_}2 is the easiest to get out, so I have chosen her for the post of honour."
10107-8{_}1204{_}As the men scattered, I heard several saying, "Good old No.
10107-8{_}1205{_}2!"
10107-8{_}1206{_}The Major told me that the Austrians were almost in Cividale, staggering news.
10107-8{_}1207{_}Tractors and lorries were to come and take away our guns and stores in the evening.
10107-8{_}1208{_}But the number of tractors was very limited and Raven was doubtful if enough would come in time.
10107-8{_}1209{_}The whole Third Army was retreating, and three British Batteries, ourselves, the Battery in Pec village and the Battery at Rupa, would be the last three Batteries of Medium or Heavy Calibre left on this part of the Front.
10107-8{_}1210{_}All through the afternoon and evening Italian Infantry and Artillery were retreating through Pec.
10107-8{_}1211{_}Some looked stolid, others depressed, others merely puzzled.
10107-8{_}1212{_}But a little later a Battalion came along the road the other way, going up to be sacrificed on Nad Logem.
10107-8{_}1213{_}They halted to rest by the roadside, full of gaiety and courage.
10107-8{_}1214{_}They cheered our men on No.
10107-8{_}1215{_}2 gun, who were pumping out shells as fast as they could.
10107-8{_}1216{_}"Bravi inglesi!"
10107-8{_}1217{_}cried the Italians, and some of our men replied, "Good luck, Johnny!"
10107-8{_}1218{_}Unknown Italians were always "Johnny."
10107-8{_}1219{_}As the dark came on, ammunition dumps began to go up everywhere; the Italians were deliberately exploding them, and great flashes of light, brighter than even an Italian noonday, lit up the whole sky for minutes at a time.
10107-8{_}1220{_}Romano's Battery next door to us threw the remains of their ammunition into the river, and pulled out and away about 6.30.
10107-8{_}1221{_}They were horse-drawn and did not need to wait for tractors.
10107-8{_}1222{_}We wished each other good-bye, and hoped we might meet again some better day.
10107-8{_}1223{_}We too got orders to destroy all ammunition we could not fire, as there would be no transport to take it away.
10107-8{_}1224{_}So we gave No.
10107-8{_}1225{_}2 a generous ration and heaved the rest into the waters of the Vippacco.
10107-8{_}1226{_}No.
10107-8{_}1227{_}2 went on firing ceaselessly.
10107-8{_}1228{_}So did one gun of the Battery in the village, and one gun at Rupa.
10107-8{_}1229{_}That Battery, being the furthest forward, was in the greatest danger of the three.
10107-8{_}1230{_}About 7 o'clock our first tractor arrived and took away No.
10107-8{_}1231{_}1 gun with Winterton and Manzoni.
10107-8{_}1232{_}Enemy bombing planes came over frequently.
10107-8{_}1233{_}One came right over us and then turned down the Vallone, and there was a series of heavy explosions, and great clouds of brownish smoke leapt up beneath her track.
10107-8{_}1234{_}Why, I kept asking myself, didn't the fools shell Pec village, where a crowd of men and guns were waiting for transport?
10107-8{_}1235{_}Why didn't they put over gas shell?
10107-8{_}1236{_}Why didn't they bomb us?
10107-8{_}1237{_}Evidently there were no Germans _here_!
10107-8{_}1238{_}About a quarter to nine No.
10107-8{_}1239{_}2 finished her ammunition, and we pulled her out.
10107-8{_}1240{_}The other three guns had gone now and the other two British Batteries were clear, all but two lorries.
10107-8{_}1241{_}Just after nine o'clock our last tractor came along and took off No.
10107-8{_}1242{_}2, with Darrell in charge of her.
10107-8{_}1243{_}How the Italians had managed to get all these lorries and tractors for us, I don't know, for, in the Third Army as a whole, they were terribly short of transport.
10107-8{_}1244{_}Many made the criticism that we should have kept out in Italy our own transport.
10107-8{_}1245{_}But the Italians certainly did us very handsomely, at the cost of losing some bigger guns of their own.
10107-8{_}1246{_}After the last British gun had ceased to fire there was for about five minutes an eerie stillness, as though all our Artillery had gone and theirs was holding its fire.
10107-8{_}1247{_}And then an Italian Field Battery opened again on the right of Pec.
10107-8{_}1248{_}For over an hour now I had been expecting, minute by minute, to see the enemy Infantry come swarming along the Nad Logem in the dusk, cutting off our retreat, for I knew we had nothing but rear-guards left up there.
10107-8{_}1249{_}But they did not come!
10107-8{_}1250{_}Only the Major and I and about forty men were left now, and we had been told that there would be no more transport.
10107-8{_}1251{_}So we destroyed everything that we had been unable to get away, and the Major informed Headquarters of the situation and then disconnected the telephone and the men fell in and we marched away.
10107-8{_}1252{_}We were just in time to see an Italian Field Battery come into Pec at the gallop, the gunners all cheering, unlimber their guns, take up position and open fire.
10107-8{_}1253{_}It was a smart piece of work, done with a real Latin gesture.
10107-8{_}1254{_}How enfuriating it was to be leaving these wooden huts of ours and these good positions, on which had been spent so many hours of labour, where we could have passed such a comfortable winter, going forth now none knew whither!
10107-8{_}1255{_}Old Natale, one of the Italians attached to us, chalked up in German on the entrance to one of the huts, "You German pigs, we shall soon be back again!"
10107-8{_}1256{_}But at that moment I did not feel so sure.
10107-8{_}1257{_}Natale was afterwards lost in the retreat, and was reported by us as "missing."
10107-8{_}1258{_}But one of our men saw him again six months later with an Italian Battery and said he looked several years younger!
10107-8{_}1259{_}We passed Campbell, the Medical Officer, standing outside his dug-out on the road.
10107-8{_}1260{_}He was waiting for the last of the other Batteries' parties to get away.
10107-8{_}1261{_}He told me afterwards that we were out only just in time.
10107-8{_}1262{_}Within half an hour of our going, the Austrians fairly plastered the position with shells of all calibres.
10107-8{_}1263{_}They shelled the road a little as we went along, but not too much.
10107-8{_}1264{_}As we passed the railway embankment at Rubbia, we saw and spoke to some Italian machine-gunners in position, whose orders were to hold up the enemy till the last possible moment.
10107-8{_}1265{_}They were quite calm and determined, those boys, knowing perfectly well that, by the time the enemy came, the Isonzo bridges would have been blown up behind them.
10107-8{_}1266{_}I dragged myself on with an aching heart.
10107-8{_}1267{_}One who retreats cuts a poor figure beside a rear-guard that stays behind and fights.
10107-8{_}1268{_}We crossed the Isonzo at Peteano, and took a short cut across the fields to Farra.
10107-8{_}1269{_}In the crowd and the dark we were jostled by some Italian Infantry.
10107-8{_}1270{_}We hailed them and found that they were our old friends, the Lecce Brigade.
10107-8{_}1271{_}The Major made our men stand back.
10107-8{_}1272{_}"Pass, Lecce," he said.
10107-8{_}1273{_}"Good luck to you!"
10107-8{_}1274{_}We marched on through Farra to Gradisca, both blazing in the night.
10107-8{_}1275{_}The towns and villages everywhere in this sector had been deliberately fired by the retreating Italians, in addition to the ammunition dumps.
10107-8{_}1276{_}The whole countryside was blazing and exploding.
10107-8{_}1277{_}I thought of Russia in 1812, and the Russian retreat before Napoleon, and Tchaikovsky's music.
10107-8{_}1278{_}It began to rain, but that made no difference to the burning.
10107-8{_}1279{_}In Gradisca burning petrol was running about the streets.
10107-8{_}1280{_}Earlier in the evening there had been a queer scene here.
10107-8{_}1281{_}The Headquarters of the British Staff had been at Gradisca, and the Camp Commandant had made a hobby of fattening rabbits for the General's Mess.
10107-8{_}1282{_}When the time had come that day to pack up and go, it was found that the lorries provided were fully loaded with office stores, Staff officers' bulky kit and 20,000 cigarettes, which the General was specially proud of having saved from his canteen.
10107-8{_}1283{_}There was no room for the Camp Commandant's rabbit hutches, so these were opened and the fat inmates released, to the delight of the civilians and Italian soldiery in Gradisca, who knocked them over or shot them as they ran.
10107-8{_}1284{_}I heard this from a gunner, who was officer's servant to one of the Staff and witnessed the scene.
10107-8{_}1285{_}A few miles away, at the Ordnance Dep√¥t at Villa Freifeldt, thousands of pounds' worth of gun stores stood ready, packed in crates, to be removed.
10107-8{_}1286{_}But no transport came for them, and they were abandoned and fell into Austrian hands.
10107-8{_}1287{_}For lack of them, our Batteries were afterwards kept out of action for several weeks.
10107-8{_}1288{_}Whoever ordered these things seems to have thought it more important to save the Staff's kit and the General's cigarettes.
10107-8{_}1289{_}Just before we entered Gradisca, we passed a Battalion of the Granatieri, the Italian Grenadiers, all six foot tall, with collar badges of crimson and white, coming up from reserve to fight a rear-guard action.
10107-8{_}1290{_}I had seen them a few days before in rest billets and admired their appearance.
10107-8{_}1291{_}And in their march that night and in their faces was scorn for fugitives and contempt for death.
10107-8{_}1292{_}The Major said to me, as they swung past us, that _that_ Battalion could be trusted to fight to the end.
10107-8{_}1293{_}And they did.
10107-8{_}1294{_}Some of our men met a few of their survivors at Mestre a week later.
10107-8{_}1295{_}Nearly the whole Battalion had been killed or wounded, but they had held up the Austrian advance for several hours.
10107-8{_}1296{_}On the further side of Gradisca we passed a great platform, which had been erected a few weeks before for the Duke of Aosta's presentation of medals for the Carso offensive.
10107-8{_}1297{_}It was here that the Major had received the Italian Silver Medal for Valour.
10107-8{_}1298{_}The platform looked ironical that night, still decked with bunting, limp and drenched now by the rain, and lit up by the flames of the burning town.
10107-8{_}1299{_}We reached Villa Viola about 11.30 p.m.
10107-8{_}1300{_}It was to have been a rendezvous, but there was no one there.
10107-8{_}1301{_}Only the rain still falling.
10107-8{_}1302{_}About midnight we entered an empty house, and threw ourselves down upon the floor to sleep.
10107-8{_}1303{_}*       *       *       *       *  We had slept for less than an hour, when we were hurriedly awakened.
10107-8{_}1304{_}The Italians had orders to set fire to the house.
10107-8{_}1305{_}Meanwhile Savogna, our Canadian Italian Corporal, had just returned from scouting for us, and reported that parties from the other Batteries were in a house half a mile away.
10107-8{_}1306{_}We marched off again through pouring rain, our path lit up by the flames, which in places thrust their long tongues right across the road.
10107-8{_}1307{_}The wind blew clouds of smoke in our faces.
10107-8{_}1308{_}The air was full of the roaring of the fires, the crackle of blazing woodwork, the crash of houses falling in, the loud explosions of ammunition dumps and petrol stores, which now and again for a few seconds lighted up the whole night sky for miles around with a terrific glare, and then died down again.
10107-8{_}1309{_}Far as the eye could reach the night was studded with red and golden fires.
10107-8{_}1310{_}Everywhere behind the front of the retreating Third Army a systematic destruction was being carried out.
10107-8{_}1311{_}The Third Army was retreating in good order, unbroken and undefeated, retreating only because its northern flank was in danger of being turned.
10107-8{_}1312{_}The Third Army was proving to the enemy that its movements were deliberate and governed by a cool purpose.
10107-8{_}1313{_}The enemy should advance into a wilderness.
10107-8{_}1314{_}Again I seemed to hear in the air the music of "1812," and the bells of burning Moscow ringing out loud and clear above the triumph song of the invader.
10107-8{_}1315{_}*       *       *       *       *  Our men marched doggedly on, some looking puzzled and full of wonder, others tired but cheerful, others with expressionless, uncomprehending faces.
10107-8{_}1316{_}But in the faces of a few I read a consciousness of the tremendous tragedy of which we formed a tiny part.
10107-8{_}1317{_}We found the other Batteries in a house not yet marked down for burning.
10107-8{_}1318{_}The house was crowded out already and all the best places taken, such as they were.
10107-8{_}1319{_}There were pools of water everywhere on the floor.
10107-8{_}1320{_}Officers of the Group were there, knowing nothing, awaiting the appearance of Colonel Raven.
10107-8{_}1321{_}All our party got in somehow and lay down to sleep.
10107-8{_}1322{_}But half an hour later we were roused again.
10107-8{_}1323{_}Raven had come and ordered that all should push on to Palmanova.
10107-8{_}1324{_}Some of our men were sleeping very heavily and were hard to waken.
10107-8{_}1325{_}When we started it was still raining.
10107-8{_}1326{_}The roads were crowded with traffic, including many guns.
10107-8{_}1327{_}Our own went by with the rest, Winterton, Darrell, Leary and Manzoni with them.
10107-8{_}1328{_}Each Battery party marched independently, the easier to get through blocks in the traffic.
10107-8{_}1329{_}The Square at Palmanova had been fixed as the next rendezvous.
10107-8{_}1330{_}The stream of refugees with their slow-moving wagons drawn by oxen, or their little donkey carts, or trudging on foot carrying bundles, became gradually thicker and more painful.
10107-8{_}1331{_}For we were back now in country that yesterday or the day before had fancied itself remote from the battle zone.
10107-8{_}1332{_}I remember one elderly peasant woman, tall and erect as a young girl, with white hair and a face like Dante, calm, beautiful and stern.
10107-8{_}1333{_}She was alone, tramping along through the mud.
10107-8{_}1334{_}And she had the walk of a queen.
10107-8{_}1335{_}At Versa we halted for a few minutes at the Hospital.
10107-8{_}1336{_}All the wounded had been evacuated.
10107-8{_}1337{_}[1] Campbell was lying on a bed in one of the empty wards, snatching a little rest.
10107-8{_}1338{_}He had seen the last British troops away from Pec and had then followed on a motor-bicycle.
10107-8{_}1339{_}I went into the old R.A.M.C.
10107-8{_}1340{_}Mess to see if any food or drink was left.
10107-8{_}1341{_}The question of food was beginning to be serious for the whole retreating Army.
10107-8{_}1342{_}Italian troops were clearing out everything.
10107-8{_}1343{_}I found a wine bottle half full, and took a deep drink.
10107-8{_}1344{_}It was vinegar, but it bucked one up.
10107-8{_}1345{_}I handed the bottle to an Italian, and told him it was "good English wine."
10107-8{_}1346{_}He drank a little, saw the joke, smiled and passed it on to an unsuspecting companion.
10107-8{_}1347{_}I got a little milk which I shared with the Major and some of our men.
10107-8{_}1348{_}Then we resumed the march.
10107-8{_}1349{_}[Footnote 1: One wounded British soldier, who had been in an Italian Field Hospital which was not evacuated in time, was taken prisoner by the Austrians.
10107-8{_}1350{_}He told me, when he was released a year later, that the Austrians bayoneted the Italian wounded whom they found in this hospital, but spared the British, and, on the whole, treated them well.]
10107-8{_}1351{_}We reached Palmanova about 7 a.m.
10107-8{_}1352{_}It was now the 28th of October.
10107-8{_}1353{_}We met Raven in the Square, where were also collected a British General and his Staff officers.
10107-8{_}1354{_}They were standing about, with a half lost look on their faces.
10107-8{_}1355{_}There was no evidence of decision or any plan.
10107-8{_}1356{_}The General was smiling, as his habit was.
10107-8{_}1357{_}The Staff Captain was telling someone, in a hopeless voice, that he had heard that the Italians were going back to the Tagliamento.
10107-8{_}1358{_}Just as we arrived, the Italians began to set fire to the town.
10107-8{_}1359{_}Dense clouds of black smoke, fanned by a strong wind, began to pour over our heads.
10107-8{_}1360{_}Flames were soon roaring round houses, where three months ago I had been a guest.
10107-8{_}1361{_}But the inmates had all gone now.
10107-8{_}1362{_}Food and drink was being sold in the shops at knock-down prices.
10107-8{_}1363{_}The Italian military authorities were requisitioning all bread, and issued some to us.
10107-8{_}1364{_}The Major ordered it to be kept in reserve.
10107-8{_}1365{_}I went round the town and into the Railway Station looking for our guns.
10107-8{_}1366{_}But there was no sign of them.
10107-8{_}1367{_}I came back and slept for an hour amid some rubble under the archway inside one of the town gates.
10107-8{_}1368{_}The town was burning furiously.
10107-8{_}1369{_}Our men, wet to the skin, sheltered themselves from the smoke and the cold wind in the dry moat outside the walls.
10107-8{_}1370{_}Then the order came to move on.
10107-8{_}1371{_}We formed up and started with the rest.
10107-8{_}1372{_}Nobody knew whither.
10107-8{_}1373{_}Some said Latisana, but no one knew how far off this was.
10107-8{_}1374{_}The men had no rations except the bread obtained at Palmanova, and no prospect, apparently, of getting any.
10107-8{_}1375{_}The Supply Officers of the A.S.C.
10107-8{_}1376{_}might as well have gone to Heaven, for all the use they were to us during those days of retreat.
10107-8{_}1377{_}It was raining again and the roads were blocked.
10107-8{_}1378{_}We proceeded slowly for a mile or two, and were then turned off the road into a damp, open field, which someone said was a "strategic point."
10107-8{_}1379{_}Here a number of different Battery parties collected.
10107-8{_}1380{_}We were to wait for the guns.
10107-8{_}1381{_}The downpour steadily increased, the field rapidly became a marsh, and there was no shelter anywhere.
10107-8{_}1382{_}Raven walked up and down, puffing at this pipe, taking the situation with admirable calm.
10107-8{_}1383{_}It was at this time that I personally touched my bedrock of misery, both mental and physical.
10107-8{_}1384{_}For there seemed to be nothing to be done, and, what most irked me, there were so many senior officers present that I myself could take no decisions.
10107-8{_}1385{_}Then some of our guns arrived, and were halted at the side of the road to wait for the rest.
10107-8{_}1386{_}But this made the traffic block worse, and they had to move forward again, and the idea of getting them all together was abandoned.
10107-8{_}1387{_}Raven then gave the order to the rest of us to move on.
10107-8{_}1388{_}There were some vacant places in various cars and lorries at this point and some footsore men were put in.
10107-8{_}1389{_}The Major insisted, in spite of my protests that I preferred to walk, that I should get into one of the cars, which I shared with Littleton, the Chaplain who had thought that war "might be tremendously worth while" and three junior officers from Raven's Headquarters.
10107-8{_}1390{_}I was, in truth, pretty done at this stage, chiefly through want of sleep, compared to which I always found want of food a trifling inconvenience.
10107-8{_}1391{_}It was now about 4 p.m. and we could only make very slow progress.
10107-8{_}1392{_}A rendezvous had been fixed by Raven at Foglie, where rations were to have been distributed.
10107-8{_}1393{_}But there was no one and no rations there, and it seemed that Raven had taken the wrong road.
10107-8{_}1394{_}The enemy were said to be advancing from the north at right angles to our only possible line of retreat, and the chances seemed strongly in favour of our all being cut off.
10107-8{_}1395{_}An Italian doctor ran out into the road and stopped our car, almost beside himself with despair.
10107-8{_}1396{_}He had been left in charge of a number of severely wounded cases, without any food, medical necessities or transport.
10107-8{_}1397{_}But we had no food and could do nothing to help him, except promise to try to have transport sent back to him from San Giorgio di Nogara.
10107-8{_}1398{_}CHAPTER XXII  FROM SAN GIORGIO TO THE TAGLIAMENTO  We reached San Giorgio about 9 p.m. and here I got out of the car, which two of Raven's Staff took on to try and arrange for transport to be sent back for the Italian wounded.
10107-8{_}1399{_}Having slept for an hour or two in the car, I felt quite a different being and fit for anything.
10107-8{_}1400{_}Stragglers were coming in from the various Batteries' dismounted parties, and I collected nearly a hundred of these men into a hall on the ground floor of an Italian Field Hospital.
10107-8{_}1401{_}They lay about on the stone floor, sleeping like logs.
10107-8{_}1402{_}Upstairs a panic had spread among the wounded that they would be abandoned.
10107-8{_}1403{_}Men were crying with terror and struggling to get out of bed.
10107-8{_}1404{_}Campbell, who had now joined us, went up and helped the Italian medical personnel.
10107-8{_}1405{_}Soon afterwards ambulances of both the Italian and British Red Cross began to arrive, and the hospital was quickly cleared.
10107-8{_}1406{_}From one British Red Cross Driver I got a large box of Cabin biscuits, which I distributed among our men, some of whom were ravenously hungry.
10107-8{_}1407{_}I also found a tap of good drinking water in the main street and here we refilled all available water bottles, including those of several men who were too fast asleep to waken.
10107-8{_}1408{_}The question then arose what to do with these stragglers.
10107-8{_}1409{_}I went to the station, but found that no more trains were running.
10107-8{_}1410{_}Latisana was said to be only "a few kilometres" away.
10107-8{_}1411{_}It was in fact more than twenty.
10107-8{_}1412{_}I discovered that it was on the Tagliamento and I supposed that, once across the river, we should be momentarily safe from risk of capture, and, if ammunition was forthcoming, our Batteries might once more come into action.
10107-8{_}1413{_}Meanwhile we should push on as soon as possible.
10107-8{_}1414{_}On the other hand the men were very tired, having been marching for twenty-four hours, with only a few short breaks.
10107-8{_}1415{_}A few hours' sleep now might be worth a lot to them later on.
10107-8{_}1416{_}Several civilians came up to me and asked when the Germans would be here.
10107-8{_}1417{_}"This is my house," one old man explained, pointing to a small house near the Hospital, "and I shall have to leave everything if I go away.
10107-8{_}1418{_}But I cannot stay....," and he began to cry.
10107-8{_}1419{_}In the early hours of the 29th I put some of our most footsore stragglers on to lorries going in the direction of Latisana.
10107-8{_}1420{_}The rest marched off under Henderson, one of the officers from Raven's Headquarters, who had come with me in the car to San Giorgio.
10107-8{_}1421{_}Meanwhile I was keeping a look-out for our guns in the dense columns of traffic slowly crawling past.
10107-8{_}1422{_}I saw guns belonging to other Batteries, and was told that some of ours were further behind.
10107-8{_}1423{_}It was just getting light, when a tractor appeared drawing two of our guns and one belonging to another British Battery, which we had picked up on the road a long way back with only three gunners in charge of it, and which would certainly have been lost, if we had not taken it in tow.
10107-8{_}1424{_}But, as the result of this additional load, our tractor had been breaking down all the way along, and had fallen almost to the rear of the retreating column.
10107-8{_}1425{_}It had a damnable and useless accumulator, but there was no means of changing this.
10107-8{_}1426{_}With the tractor and guns were Winterton, Darrell, and Leary, also the Battery Quartermaster Sergeant and two of our lorries.
10107-8{_}1427{_}They told me Manzoni was well on ahead with the other two guns and I told them that the Major and the bulk of the dismounted party must also be a good distance ahead, as stragglers from this party had appeared here many hours before.
10107-8{_}1428{_}We were now the last British guns on the road, a post of honour which we continued to hold.
10107-8{_}1429{_}I was delighted to find that I was now entitled, by reason of seniority, to take command.
10107-8{_}1430{_}I sent on the two lorries with Winterton and Darrell, to get in touch as soon as possible with the two guns in front and the Major's party.
10107-8{_}1431{_}Leary and I remained behind with the tractor and its load.
10107-8{_}1432{_}We had about thirty men with us and a small quantity of rations, including a little tea.
10107-8{_}1433{_}We moved on slowly and got stuck in a bad block of traffic at San Giorgio cross roads.
10107-8{_}1434{_}Here we had to remain stationary for several hours.
10107-8{_}1435{_}The dawn was breaking and we made some tea.
10107-8{_}1436{_}About 5 a.m.
10107-8{_}1437{_}I got tired of sitting still and walked about half a mile down the road to find out the cause of the block.
10107-8{_}1438{_}I began to control and jerrymander the traffic and at first annoyed an Italian officer, who was there with the same object as myself; but I persuasively pointed out to him the benefits to both of us, if we could only succeed in getting a move on, and he then calmed down and began to help me.
10107-8{_}1439{_}In the end we both manoeuvred our own transport into a moving stream, and went forward smiling.
10107-8{_}1440{_}We went along at a fine pace for several miles and then our tractor stopped and wouldn't start up again.
10107-8{_}1441{_}Whereupon there came to our assistance a young man named Rinaldo Rinaldi, a skilled and resourceful mechanic, who was driving a tractor in rear of us.
10107-8{_}1442{_}He patched up our engine and got us going again.
10107-8{_}1443{_}But we kept on breaking down after intervals never very long.
10107-8{_}1444{_}Time after time Rinaldo Rinaldi came running up, smiling and eager to help.
10107-8{_}1445{_}He patched us up and got us going six times.
10107-8{_}1446{_}But at last he had to pass us and go on.
10107-8{_}1447{_}For he, too, was drawing guns.
10107-8{_}1448{_}I shall never forget Rinaldo Rinaldi and the cheerful help he gave us.
10107-8{_}1449{_}In the end he left us an accumulator, but it was not much better than our own.
10107-8{_}1450{_}Enemy planes now began to appear in the sky, some scouting only, others dropping bombs.
10107-8{_}1451{_}They did more damage to the wretched refugees than to the military.
10107-8{_}1452{_}What chances they missed that day!
10107-8{_}1453{_}Once or twice, when we were stationary, I gave the order to scatter in the fields to left and right of the road.
10107-8{_}1454{_}But they never came very near to hitting us.
10107-8{_}1455{_}They flew very high and their markmanship was atrocious.
10107-8{_}1456{_}Atrocious also was our tractor!
10107-8{_}1457{_}Finally, when it broke down and we had no fresh accumulator, we had to unlimber the front gun, attach drag ropes to the tractor, haul vigorously on the ropes until the engine started up, then back the tractor and front limber back to the guns, limber up, cast off the ropes and go ahead again.
10107-8{_}1458{_}We did this three or four times in the course of an hour, and enjoyed the sense of triumphing over obstacles.
10107-8{_}1459{_}But it was very laborious, and the intervals between successive breakdowns grew ominously shorter and shorter.
10107-8{_}1460{_}And the last time the trick didn't work, though we had all heaved and heaved till we were very near exhaustion.
10107-8{_}1461{_}We were fairly stuck now, half blocking the road.
10107-8{_}1462{_}Great excitement, as was only natural, developed among those behind us.
10107-8{_}1463{_}I sent forward an orderly with a message to the Major, describing our plight and asking that, if possible, another tractor might be sent back from Latisana to pull us.
10107-8{_}1464{_}This message never reached the Major, but was opened by another Field officer, who sent back this flatulent reply.
10107-8{_}1465{_}"If you are with Major Blinks, you had better ask him whether you may use your own discretion and, if necessary, remove breech blocks and abandon guns."
10107-8{_}1466{_}I was not with Major Blinks, and I neither knew nor cared where he might be.
10107-8{_}1467{_}Nor had I any intention of abandoning the guns.
10107-8{_}1468{_}I determined, without asking anyone's permission, to use my discretion in a different way.
10107-8{_}1469{_}I saw, a little distance in front, an Italian Field Artillery Colonel in a state of wild excitement.
10107-8{_}1470{_}He was rushing about with an unopened bottle of red wine in his hand, waving it ferociously at the heads of refugees, and driving them and their carts off the road down a side track.
10107-8{_}1471{_}A queer pathetic freight some of these carts carried, marble clocks and blankets, big wine flasks and canaries in cages.
10107-8{_}1472{_}The Colonel had driven off the road also a certain Captain Medola, of whom I shall have more to say in a moment, and who was sitting sulkily on his horse among the civilian carts.
10107-8{_}1473{_}The Colonel's object, it appeared, was to get a number of Field Batteries through.
10107-8{_}1474{_}He had cleared a gap in the blocked traffic and his Field Guns were now streaming past at a sharp trot.
10107-8{_}1475{_}But he was an extraordinary spectacle and made me want to laugh.
10107-8{_}1476{_}Treading very delicately, I approached this enfuriated man, and explained the helpless situation of our guns, pointing out that we were also unwillingly impeding the movements of his own.
10107-8{_}1477{_}I asked if he could order any transport to be provided for us.
10107-8{_}1478{_}He waved his bottle at me, showed no sign of either civility or comprehension, only screaming at the top of his voice, "Va via, va via!
10107-8{_}1479{_}"[1]  [Footnote 1: "Away with you, away with you!"]
10107-8{_}1480{_}I gave him up as hopeless, and went back to my guns, intending to wait till he had disappeared and things had quieted down again, and then to look for help elsewhere.
10107-8{_}1481{_}But the Latin mind often follows a thread of order through what an Anglo-Saxon is apt to mistake for a mere hurricane of confused commotion.
10107-8{_}1482{_}Within five minutes Captain Medola came up to me and said that the Colonel had ordered him to drag our tractor and guns.
10107-8{_}1483{_}Medola was in command of a Battery of long guns, and had one of these attached to a powerful tractor on the road in front of us.
10107-8{_}1484{_}To this long gun, therefore, we now attached our tractor, useless as a tractor but containing valuable gun stores, and our three guns.
10107-8{_}1485{_}It was a tremendous strain for one tractor, however strong, to pull, and we decided a little later to abandon our own tractor and most of its contents.
10107-8{_}1486{_}Medola, having handed over his horse to an orderly, who was to ride on ahead and arrange for a fresh supply of petrol for his tractors, of which there were three, mounted the front of the leading tractor and I got up beside him.
10107-8{_}1487{_}He rendered us most invaluable help in a most willing spirit and at considerable risk to himself.
10107-8{_}1488{_}For he undoubtedly had to go much more slowly with us in tow than he could have gone if he had been alone.
10107-8{_}1489{_}We saw another Battery of Italian heavy guns going along the road, heavier than either ours or Medola's.
10107-8{_}1490{_}They were an ancient type, which we had seen sometimes on the Carso, and not of very high military value.
10107-8{_}1491{_}But their gunners took a regimental and affectionate pride in those old guns.
10107-8{_}1492{_}They had neither tractors nor horses, but they had dragged their beloved pieces for thirty miles from the rocky heights of the Carso, along good roads and bad, up and down hill, through impossible traffic blocks, down on to the plains as far as Palmanova, with nothing but long ropes and their own strong arms.
10107-8{_}1493{_}They had forty men hauling on each gun.
10107-8{_}1494{_}At Palmanova new hauling parties had been put on, who dragged the guns another thirty miles to the far side of the Tagliamento at Latisana.
10107-8{_}1495{_}And as they hauled, they sang, until they were too tired to go on singing, and could only raise, from time to time, their rhythmical periodic cry of "Sforza!...
10107-8{_}1496{_}Sforza!
10107-8{_}1497{_}"[1]  [Footnote 1: "Heave!...
10107-8{_}1498{_}Heave!"]
10107-8{_}1499{_}As we passed through Muzzano, the town and road were heavily bombed.
10107-8{_}1500{_}The bell in the campanile jangled wildly and weeping women crowded into the church, as though thinking to find sanctuary there.
10107-8{_}1501{_}Others stood gazing helplessly up into the sky.
10107-8{_}1502{_}Here I saw some Italian Infantry, mostly young, who were delighted to be retreating.
10107-8{_}1503{_}"Forward, you militarists!"
10107-8{_}1504{_}they cried to us as we passed.
10107-8{_}1505{_}"This is your punishment!
10107-8{_}1506{_}How much longer do you think the war is going to last?
10107-8{_}1507{_}What about Trieste now?"
10107-8{_}1508{_}They spoke with joyful irony, as though the conquest of Trieste had been a slaves' task, imposed upon unwilling Italy by foreign imperialists.
10107-8{_}1509{_}They were the only Italian troops I saw during the retreat, who showed any sign of being under the influence of "defeatist" or German propaganda.
10107-8{_}1510{_}The stream of refugees steadily thickened on the roads.
10107-8{_}1511{_}More than once I got down and ran on ahead, calling out with monotonous refrain to the drivers of civilian carts to keep well over to the right of the road, so as to let the guns pass.
10107-8{_}1512{_}They all did their best to obey, poor brutes, and we gained some useful ground in that endless column.
10107-8{_}1513{_}At nightfall we were still eight or nine kilometres from Latisana.
10107-8{_}1514{_}The traffic block grew worse and worse, and there were too few Carabinieri to exercise proper control.
10107-8{_}1515{_}We stuck for hours at a time, with nothing moving for miles, three motionless lines of traffic abreast on the road, all pointing in the same direction.
10107-8{_}1516{_}Tired men slept and wakeful men waited and watched and cursed at the delay.
10107-8{_}1517{_}Behind us, far off, we could hear the booming of the guns, which seemed from hour to hour to come a little nearer, and flashes of distant gunfire flickered in the night sky.
10107-8{_}1518{_}Back there the rear-guards were still fighting, and brave men were dying to give us time to get away.
10107-8{_}1519{_}It seemed just then that their sacrifice might be in vain.
10107-8{_}1520{_}What a haul the Austrians would have here!
10107-8{_}1521{_}And behind and around us burning villages were still flaming in the dark, and throwing up the sharp black outlines of the trees.
10107-8{_}1522{_}*       *       *       *       *  Afterwards I heard of some of the deeds that had been done "back there."
10107-8{_}1523{_}I heard of the charges of the Italian Cavalry, of the Novara Lancers and the Genoa Dragoons, crack regiments, full of the best horsemen in Italy, who had been waiting, waiting, all the war through, for their chance to come.
10107-8{_}1524{_}Their chance had come at last, the chance to die, charging against overwhelming odds, in order that Italy, or at least the glory of her name, might live for ever.
10107-8{_}1525{_}One commanding officer called all his officers around him and said, "The common people of Italy have betrayed our country's honour, and now we, the gentlemen of Italy, are going to save it!"
10107-8{_}1526{_}and then he led the charge, and fell leading it.
10107-8{_}1527{_}It was a fine, aristocratic gesture, though the prejudices of his class partly blinded him.
10107-8{_}1528{_}Near Cervignano Italian Cavalry charged the massed machine guns of the enemy and, when the horses went down, the men went on, and then the men went down, all but a few, and those few for a moment broke the line and held up the advance, and gave to the mass of the retreating troops just that little space of extra time, which spans the gulf between escape and destruction.
10107-8{_}1529{_}And away up north on Monte Nero, left behind when the rest of the Army retired, Alpini and Bersaglieri resisted for many days, and aeroplanes flew back and dropped food and ammunition from the skies for them.
10107-8{_}1530{_}And when their ammunition was all shot away, that garrison came down into the plains, and a few survivors fought their way through with bombs and bayonets back to the Italian lines.
10107-8{_}1531{_}And many other such deeds were surely done that will never be known, because the men that did them died out of sight of any of their comrades who survived.
10107-8{_}1532{_}*       *       *       *       *  In the small hours of the 30th of October, I left our guns in Leary's charge and determined to walk on to Latisana, to see if I could not find some person in authority and get something done to move things on.
10107-8{_}1533{_}I had only gone a little way when I met Bixio, a Captain of Mountain Artillery, attached to Raven's Headquarters.
10107-8{_}1534{_}He had come back to see how far behind our rearmost guns were.
10107-8{_}1535{_}I saw him several times during the retreat.
10107-8{_}1536{_}He did fine work more than once in creating order out of confusion.
10107-8{_}1537{_}He looked a magnificent, almost a Mephistophelian, figure, with his dark features, his flashing angry eyes, his air of decision, his sharp gestures, his tall body enveloped in a loose cloak, his Alpino hat, with its long single feather.
10107-8{_}1538{_}He told me that all traffic along this road into Latisana had been stopped for the past three hours, in order to let traffic from the north get on, for it was from that direction that the advance of the enemy was most threatening.
10107-8{_}1539{_}I walked on and found a British Red Cross Ambulance stuck in the block.
10107-8{_}1540{_}I talked for a few moments to the driver, who gave me a piece of cake and some wine.
10107-8{_}1541{_}When I reached Latisana, I found traffic pouring through along the road from the north.
10107-8{_}1542{_}I crossed the bridge over the Tagliamento and looked down at the broad swift current, glistening beneath.
10107-8{_}1543{_}Hope leapt again within me at the sight.
10107-8{_}1544{_}Here, at last, I said to myself, is a fine natural obstacle.
10107-8{_}1545{_}We shall turn here and stand at bay, and the invader will come no further.
10107-8{_}1546{_}I had been told that there were some huts on the right hand side, just over the bridge, where our men would be, where the A.S.C.
10107-8{_}1547{_}would have delivered rations and the Staff had fixed a rendezvous.
10107-8{_}1548{_}I, therefore, expected to find the Major and our dismounted party, or at least someone from another Battery, or some of either Raven's or the General's Staff.
10107-8{_}1549{_}But there was nothing there; no British troops, no rations, and no Staff!
10107-8{_}1550{_}Only the never ending rain, and a confused stream of Italian troops, chiefly Field Guns, hurrying across the bridge.
10107-8{_}1551{_}There was nothing to do but to go back.
10107-8{_}1552{_}The sentries on the bridge tried to stop me, but I insisted that I must see some Artillery officer in authority.
10107-8{_}1553{_}They directed me to the Square, where I found Colonel Canale, controlling the movements of Batteries, looking straight before him out of uncomprehending, heavy eyes, like one crushed under a weight of bitter humiliation.
10107-8{_}1554{_}He asked where our guns were.
10107-8{_}1555{_}I told him they were getting near now, but stuck fast in the traffic.
10107-8{_}1556{_}He said it was forbidden to let through traffic on that road at present, but he would do what he could.
10107-8{_}1557{_}I asked if there were any new orders.
10107-8{_}1558{_}"No," he said, "only forward across the bridge, and then push on as fast as possible to Portogruaro."
10107-8{_}1559{_}I left him, and found three of our stragglers from the Major's party, asleep on the floor of a forge.
10107-8{_}1560{_}I told them to cross the river and wait on the Portogruaro road for myself and the guns.
10107-8{_}1561{_}I asked an Italian Corporal if there was anywhere in Latisana where one could get a drink.
10107-8{_}1562{_}He said he thought not, but gave me a bottle full of cold coffee, brandy and sugar in about equal proportions.
10107-8{_}1563{_}It was a splendid drink, but a little too sweet.
10107-8{_}1564{_}I walked back along the road towards the guns.
10107-8{_}1565{_}Some houses on the outskirts of the town were burning furiously.
10107-8{_}1566{_}The traffic was beginning to move forward along our road, very slowly and with frequent halts.
10107-8{_}1567{_}I had two overcoats with me when we started from Pec.
10107-8{_}1568{_}Both were long ago wet through, and I was wearing over my shoulders at this time a blanket lent to me by Medola.
10107-8{_}1569{_}This, too, was thoroughly drenched by now.
10107-8{_}1570{_}In the fields on either side of the road Infantry were lying out in the rain, asleep, dreaming, perhaps, of Rome or Sicily or the Bay of Naples.
10107-8{_}1571{_}The dawn of another day was breaking, cold, damp and miserable, symbolic of this great weary tragedy.
10107-8{_}1572{_}*       *       *       *       *  I had not gone far when I met four of our men carrying on a stretcher the dead body of the Battery Staff Sergeant Artificer.
10107-8{_}1573{_}He had dropped asleep on one of the guns and, as the tractor moved on, he had fallen forward, head downwards, beneath the gun wheel, which had passed over him, along the whole length of his body, crushing him to death.
10107-8{_}1574{_}They said he died before they could get him out.
10107-8{_}1575{_}He was a good man and a very skilled worker, full of pluck and spirit.
10107-8{_}1576{_}The last thing he had done for me was to get everything ready for rendering the guns unserviceable in case we should have to abandon them.
10107-8{_}1577{_}There was no chance of decent burial for him here, but I had his body placed upon an empty trench cart, which was being towed by a lorry of another Battery, and put two of our men in charge of it.
10107-8{_}1578{_}They buried him the next day or the day after in a cemetery near Portogruaro.
10107-8{_}1579{_}About 7 a.m., as I was still making my way back through the traffic towards our guns, it was reported that enemy cavalry patrols had been seen to the north of the road, and that shots had been exchanged.
10107-8{_}1580{_}For a moment there was some panic and confusion, but a scheme of defence was quickly organised.
10107-8{_}1581{_}No one had supposed that they could yet be so near.
10107-8{_}1582{_}I found Bixio rallying some Infantrymen, with eloquent words and great gestures, and an Italian Infantry Major, calm and smiling, was putting out a screen of machine gunners and riflemen across the road itself and along a hedge five hundred yards to the north of it.
10107-8{_}1583{_}All was in readiness for putting our guns completely out of action.
10107-8{_}1584{_}There would be nothing else to do, if the enemy appeared, for we had no gun ammunition, and it was impossible to get on, until the whole traffic block in front of us had been shifted forward.
10107-8{_}1585{_}But I told Bixio that I should do nothing to the guns, unless there was some evidence that the enemy was really approaching with a superiority of force over our own.
10107-8{_}1586{_}The enemy, however, did not at that time reappear and the best bit of hustling traffic management that I had yet witnessed during the retreat, now took place.
10107-8{_}1587{_}The northern road was at last clear at Latisana, and the authorities turned their attention to us.
10107-8{_}1588{_}A breakdown gang appeared and a number of new tractors and lorries with refills of petrol.
10107-8{_}1589{_}Civilian carts whose drivers remained, were ordered to drive on, those which had been abandoned were overturned to one side into the ditches, and dead horses and wreckage due to bombing or the brief moments of panic were likewise thrust off the road.
10107-8{_}1590{_}Relays of fresh drivers took over all the lorries and tractors which would still go.
10107-8{_}1591{_}The rest went into the ditch on top of the dead horses and derelict carts.
10107-8{_}1592{_}The heavier loads which single tractors had been pulling were split up between two or more.
10107-8{_}1593{_}In a surprisingly short time the whole mass began to move.
10107-8{_}1594{_}Here I parted from Medola, who had been a very good friend to us.
10107-8{_}1595{_}Our three guns got a new tractor to themselves and I got up beside the driver.
10107-8{_}1596{_}And so at last we entered Latisana.
10107-8{_}1597{_}Our new driver was immensely enthusiastic, but very excited.
10107-8{_}1598{_}He told me that he had had two brothers killed in the war and had applied, when the retreat began, to be transferred from Mechanical Transport to the Infantry.
10107-8{_}1599{_}That morning, he said, he had heard General Pettiti, who was our Army Corps Commander, give the order that all the British Batteries must first be got across the river and only then the Italian.
10107-8{_}1600{_}I said that I saw no good reason for this preference, but that anyhow he was driving the last three British guns.
10107-8{_}1601{_}This pleased him tremendously.
10107-8{_}1602{_}By now I was wrapped up in a new and dry Italian blanket, which I had taken from an abandoned cart by the roadside.
10107-8{_}1603{_}Our tractor, less enthusiastic than its driver, broke down continually.
10107-8{_}1604{_}It was rumoured that the bridge had been blown up already, and there were wild screams of despair from a crowd of women, who came running past us.
10107-8{_}1605{_}At last we turned the last corner and came in sight of the Tagliamento.
10107-8{_}1606{_}The bridge was still intact.
10107-8{_}1607{_}Italian Generals were rushing to and fro, gesticulating, giving orders.
10107-8{_}1608{_}General Pettiti sent a special orderly to ask me if mine were the last British guns.
10107-8{_}1609{_}I told him yes.
10107-8{_}1610{_}Our tractor broke down three times on the bridge itself.
10107-8{_}1611{_}But at last we were over.
10107-8{_}1612{_}One of our party had an Italian flag and waved it and cried "Viva l'Italia!"
10107-8{_}1613{_}Not long after, the bridge went up, with an explosion that could be heard for miles around.
10107-8{_}1614{_}CHAPTER XXIII  FROM THE TAGLIAMENTO TO TREVISO  I heard later that the Major and his party had reached Latisana the previous day.
10107-8{_}1615{_}Winterton had joined them near Muzzano.
10107-8{_}1616{_}They had marched for forty-eight hours practically without food and with only some three hours' rest in stray halts.
10107-8{_}1617{_}They had been magnificent, but they were utterly done, and the Major, who had been most done of all, told me afterwards that it had made him cry to watch them hobbling along,--some of them men too old or of too low a medical category to have passed for the Infantry,--and to hear them singing,     "What's the use of worrying?
10107-8{_}1618{_}It never was worth while.
10107-8{_}1619{_}So pack up your sorrows in your old kit bag,     And smile, smile, smile!"
10107-8{_}1620{_}The spirit of the men in the retreat from Mons was not finer than the spirit of those men of ours.
10107-8{_}1621{_}At Latisana they got on board a train for Treviso.
10107-8{_}1622{_}It was about the last train that was running.
10107-8{_}1623{_}*       *       *       *       *  My party, though they were longer on the road, were at least able to ride a great part of the way on the tractors and guns.
10107-8{_}1624{_}Once across the Tagliamento, our tractor not only continued to break down every few hundred yards, but also developed the unpleasant habit of catching fire.
10107-8{_}1625{_}Twice we put the fire out with the squirts and chemicals provided for the purpose, and a third time with mud.
10107-8{_}1626{_}I determined not to risk a fourth time, and so pulled on to the side of the road and halted.
10107-8{_}1627{_}I sent on the Battery Sergeant Major on a passing lorry to Portogruaro with a note to the Major asking that another tractor might be sent back, and I also sent Avoglia to the nearest Italian Headquarters to see if he could raise a tractor there.
10107-8{_}1628{_}We were halted at the top of a hill on the road running along the western bank of the river.
10107-8{_}1629{_}We were indeed literally "across," but we should have provided a splendid target for enemy Artillery advancing on the further side.
10107-8{_}1630{_}A good system of trenches ran alongside the road, and these were now manned in force by Italian Infantry.
10107-8{_}1631{_}Field Guns also had come into position behind them.
10107-8{_}1632{_}Our men took advantage of the enforced halt to collect fuel, light fires and make tea.
10107-8{_}1633{_}We were still halted here at nightfall.
10107-8{_}1634{_}Soon after dark some Italians came up and told us that we were blocking the road, which was not true, as we were well to the side.
10107-8{_}1635{_}However, as neither Avoglia nor the Sergeant Major had yet returned with a new tractor, and as the Italians said that they would pull us on, I cordially agreed to the attempt being made.
10107-8{_}1636{_}They attached a tractor with a heavy lorry in tow to our inflammatory tractor and our three guns.
10107-8{_}1637{_}They asked that an attempt should be made to start up our tractor also, but I succeeded in persuading them that this was inexpedient.
10107-8{_}1638{_}They then started up their own tractor only.
10107-8{_}1639{_}To my great surprise, we began to move.
10107-8{_}1640{_}It was a magnificent machine, and forged ahead splendidly, contrary to all the laws limiting its capacity, rumbling and backfiring under the unwonted strain, for miles through the gloom.
10107-8{_}1641{_}Then the moon began to rise.
10107-8{_}1642{_}The night, for the first time since the retreat began, was fine and clear.
10107-8{_}1643{_}We could only go slowly and broke down now and then.
10107-8{_}1644{_}But all went pretty well, until we swung our long train a little too sharply round a corner in the road, and the last two guns got ditched.
10107-8{_}1645{_}While we were trying to get them out, a British Major, whom I will call Star, appeared on the scene.
10107-8{_}1646{_}He came from Portogruaro with the news that five new tractors were on their way back, and that some other British guns were ditched further ahead.
10107-8{_}1647{_}I therefore thanked the officer in charge of the Italian tractor and lorry for all he had done for us and advised him now to go on and leave us, as our position was tiresome but no longer critical.
10107-8{_}1648{_}This he did.
10107-8{_}1649{_}The moonlight was now bright as day, and one of Star's promised tractors arrived and finally succeeded in getting out our ditched guns.
10107-8{_}1650{_}*       *       *       *       *  Star had painted a bright picture of Portogruaro.
10107-8{_}1651{_}All the British guns, he said, were parked together in the Piazza and there was a large granary close by, full of happy men with plenty of rations and straw.
10107-8{_}1652{_}So, it seems, some imaginative person had told him.
10107-8{_}1653{_}We reached Portogruaro in the small hours of the 31st of October.
10107-8{_}1654{_}The moon had set and it was very dark.
10107-8{_}1655{_}Several of us made a most careful search in the Piazza.
10107-8{_}1656{_}But there were no British guns there, no granary, no straw, no rations.
10107-8{_}1657{_}I halted the guns just outside the gate of the town and told the men to turn in and sleep.
10107-8{_}1658{_}Soon after daybreak we all woke feeling very hungry.
10107-8{_}1659{_}I issued practically all that remained of our rations, a little bully, a little biscuit and a very little tea.
10107-8{_}1660{_}Wanting a wash and, still more urgently, a shave, I went into a house and asked for the loan of some soap and a towel.
10107-8{_}1661{_}A number of terrified old women gathered round me, in doubt whether to fly or to stay.
10107-8{_}1662{_}I advised them to stay, for I took for granted at this time that the Tagliamento line would hold.
10107-8{_}1663{_}They pressed upon me coffee and bread, and I heard them repeating over and over again to one another my assurances that the enemy was still far away and would never get as far as Portogruaro.
10107-8{_}1664{_}It was hard not to cry.
10107-8{_}1665{_}Star arrived during the morning and took charge.
10107-8{_}1666{_}There was no need, he said, to hurry on.
10107-8{_}1667{_}We had better rest here for a day.
10107-8{_}1668{_}He arranged for us all to draw rations from the Italian Comando di Tappa.
10107-8{_}1669{_}Treviso was to be our next stopping place.
10107-8{_}1670{_}We were disturbed a little during the morning by enemy planes dropping bombs on the town, but none fell very near us.
10107-8{_}1671{_}In the afternoon we moved on and parked our guns near the station along with those of the other British Batteries, which had arrived before us.
10107-8{_}1672{_}Bombing raids continued and were more serious that afternoon than in the morning.
10107-8{_}1673{_}One bomb fell on a house, which was full of men from one of the other Batteries, and caused a number of casualties.
10107-8{_}1674{_}It was only by good luck that a number of my own men were not in that house at the time.
10107-8{_}1675{_}Fortunately I had had words, as two tired men will, with one of the officers of the other Battery, about the joint use of the kitchen, and my men, when I asked them, had decided that they preferred, as always, to "run their own show" and not "pig in with other Batteries."
10107-8{_}1676{_}To that attitude of independence some of them probably owe their lives.
10107-8{_}1677{_}In the afternoon Raven turned up, and said that he had arranged for us to go on to Treviso by train.
10107-8{_}1678{_}We loaded our guns on to trucks, and waited several hours in the station yard for the promised train.
10107-8{_}1679{_}It was cold and wet and more bombers came over us.
10107-8{_}1680{_}They had bombed the station for the last three nights, I heard.
10107-8{_}1681{_}But nothing hit it while we were there.
10107-8{_}1682{_}The train left at 9.30 p.m. Leary and another officer and I tried to share one wet blanket.
10107-8{_}1683{_}We were too wet and cold to sleep.
10107-8{_}1684{_}I walked up and down the carriage trying to get warm.
10107-8{_}1685{_}They bombed the railway several times during our journey, and once, when a bomb fell near our train, there was a rumour that the engine driver had gone away and left us standing.
10107-8{_}1686{_}But it was quite untrue.
10107-8{_}1687{_}We crawled along, with many stops.
10107-8{_}1688{_}It seemed a quite interminable journey.
10107-8{_}1689{_}But at 8 o'clock next morning, the 1st of November, we came to Treviso.
10107-8{_}1690{_}CHAPTER XXIV  THOUGHTS AFTER THE DISASTER  We hung about for a while in the station, nobody knowing what was to happen next.
10107-8{_}1691{_}Then Leary and I went off to try to find some food.
10107-8{_}1692{_}We had been living just lately on ration biscuits and a tin of Australian peach jam.
10107-8{_}1693{_}There was not much left at the Buffet, where we found Bixio, but we got a little salami and some eels and wine and coffee.
10107-8{_}1694{_}Meanwhile our train had gone on to Mestre, owing to a mistake between two railway officials, and had to return next day.
10107-8{_}1695{_}Leary's feet were so bad that he could hardly walk.
10107-8{_}1696{_}I got them dressed for him by the Italian Red Cross, but he could walk no better afterwards.
10107-8{_}1697{_}The Villa Passi, the British Headquarters, was several miles off.
10107-8{_}1698{_}An enemy plane came over and bombed Treviso, when we were in the station square, trying in vain to find a conveyance.
10107-8{_}1699{_}But none of the bombs fell very close to us.
10107-8{_}1700{_}At last we hailed a British lorry, which took us to Villa Passi, and then on to Carbonera, where odds and ends of Batteries had been turning up for several days past.
10107-8{_}1701{_}The Major was very delighted to see us, a rumour having got about that we and the last guns had been left on the wrong side of the Tagliamento, when the bridge went up.
10107-8{_}1702{_}He had almost given up hope of seeing us again.
10107-8{_}1703{_}Then I went to bed and slept for hours and hours.
10107-8{_}1704{_}Next morning from my window I could see the Alps lying very low on the horizon, like a ball of fluffy snow.
10107-8{_}1705{_}The sun was shining and a fountain was playing in the garden.
10107-8{_}1706{_}I could hardly realise that we had reached, for a moment at least, a place of peace, where there was no more fighting or retreating.
10107-8{_}1707{_}Our men were worn out, most of them, and slept like logs.
10107-8{_}1708{_}They had been sorely tried.
10107-8{_}1709{_}Their pluck and endurance had been splendid.
10107-8{_}1710{_}But they got no message of thanks or praise from the British General who at that time nominally commanded us.
10107-8{_}1711{_}This distinguished man I had last seen in the Square at Palmanova, amid the smoke and flames, with his car standing close at hand ready to push off, and he had arrived at Treviso in good time.
10107-8{_}1712{_}He was now comfortably installed at the Villa Passi, and the day some of our footsore men limped into Treviso, he was lunching with his Staff, all bright and polished and sleek, in the Hotel Stella d'Oro.
10107-8{_}1713{_}We all expected, for days, that he would call a parade and address the men who had saved what he used to call "his guns," or at least that he would send some message.
10107-8{_}1714{_}But he made no sign, except to open a canteen for the sale of the 20,000 cigarettes, which some intelligent subordinate had saved in preference to valuable gun stores now in Austrian hands.
10107-8{_}1715{_}*       *       *       *       *  The day after my arrival I read a newspaper for the first time for over a week, but the news was very bad and the retreat still continuing.
10107-8{_}1716{_}The Austrians were across the Tagliamento in strong force at several points.
10107-8{_}1717{_}I tried to reason and make distinctions, but my brain was still too tired to answer the helm, so I left it.
10107-8{_}1718{_}We ate hot polenta and drank wonderful coffee, having established our Battery Mess in the porter's lodge at the entrance to the Villa Lebreton, and persuaded the porter's wife to cook for us.
10107-8{_}1719{_}All the Battery had discovered the polenta at the porter's lodge and our men crowded the kitchen at all hours of the day.
10107-8{_}1720{_}We all appreciated good food after the short rations of the retreat.
10107-8{_}1721{_}Conversation was intensely depressing when not utterly trivial.
10107-8{_}1722{_}I remember walking round and round the vegetable garden at the back of the Villa with an Italian friend of mine, trying both to face the facts and to draw some comfort from them.
10107-8{_}1723{_}It was an impossible task.
10107-8{_}1724{_}My friend was full of despair and bitterness.
10107-8{_}1725{_}"The fruits of thirty months of war all lost in two days," he said, "and much more lost besides!
10107-8{_}1726{_}What will all the mothers think, who have lost sons on San Michele and Monte Santo?
10107-8{_}1727{_}It is a common thing in Italy now for families to have lost four or five sons.
10107-8{_}1728{_}What will the mothers of Italy think of this?
10107-8{_}1729{_}Would not any of them be justified in shooting Cadorna?
10107-8{_}1730{_}The Third Army should not have been ordered to retire.
10107-8{_}1731{_}They should have counter-attacked instead.
10107-8{_}1732{_}But now would it not be better to make peace at once?
10107-8{_}1733{_}Is there no man who will rise up and say, 'Stop, stop, stop this bloody business now, before it gets any worse?'
10107-8{_}1734{_}Some of our soldiers looked quite pleased to be retreating.
10107-8{_}1735{_}Poor children!
10107-8{_}1736{_}They thought the war was over and they were going home.
10107-8{_}1737{_}There is a frightful danger that the leaders,--the generals and the politicians at Rome,--will say 'fight on!'
10107-8{_}1738{_}but the rank and file will go on breaking.
10107-8{_}1739{_}'We are fighting for Trento and Trieste!'
10107-8{_}1740{_}they used to say, and now they say 'we are organising the defence of the Piave line!'
10107-8{_}1741{_}The Regular soldiers never want the war to end.
10107-8{_}1742{_}And soon they will be distributing medals for the retreat.
10107-8{_}1743{_}Medals!"
10107-8{_}1744{_}I could find no words worth saying to him in reply.
