10107-8{_}2332{_}On the northern side of the Plateau rose a formidable mountain range, the chief heights of which, from west to east, were Monte Campolungo, Monte Erio, Monte Mosciagh and Monte Longara.
10107-8{_}2333{_}This range was thickly wooded with pines, among which our guns did great damage.
10107-8{_}2334{_}I always more regretted the destruction of trees than of uninhabited houses, for the latter can be the more quickly replaced.
10107-8{_}2335{_}This range was pierced by only four valleys, through each of which ran roads vital to the Austrian system of communications, the Val Campomulo, the Val di Nos, the Val d'Assa and the Val di Martello.
10107-8{_}2336{_}The Austrians had also a few roads over the top of the mountains, but these were less good and less convenient.
10107-8{_}2337{_}Along the southern side of the Plateau ran another ridge, less mountainous than the ridge to the north, and completely in our possession.
10107-8{_}2338{_}This ridge also was thickly wooded, and pierced by only a few valleys and roads.
10107-8{_}2339{_}The road we came to know best was the continuation of the wonderful road up from the plain, through Granezza to the cross-roads at Pria dell' Acqua, and on through the Baerenthal Valley to San Sisto.
10107-8{_}2340{_}Thence it led through the front line trenches into the town of Asiago itself.
10107-8{_}2341{_}At Pria dell' Acqua, a most misleading name, where there was no water, but only a collection of wooden huts, another road branched off westwards, running parallel to the front line, behind the southern ridge of the Plateau.
10107-8{_}2342{_}The Italian Engineers had created a magnificent network of roads in this sector of the Front.
10107-8{_}2343{_}Before the war there had been only one road into Asiago from the plain.
10107-8{_}2344{_}Now there were half a dozen, all broad and with a fine surface, capable of taking any traffic.
10107-8{_}2345{_}And, in addition, there were many transverse roads, equally good, joining up and cutting across the main routes at convenient points.
10107-8{_}2346{_}When the British troops took over this sector in March, the whole Plateau, properly so called, was in Austrian hands.
10107-8{_}2347{_}It had been taken last November in the mountain offensive which followed Caporetto.
10107-8{_}2348{_}At one perilous moment the Austrians had held San Sisto and their patrols had passed Pria dell' Acqua, but they had been thrown back by Italian counter-attacks to the line they now held.
10107-8{_}2349{_}Our front line ran along the southern edge of the Plateau, and, on the right, along the lower slopes of the southern ridge, just inside the pine woods.
10107-8{_}2350{_}On the left, further west, it ran mostly on the flat and more in the open.
10107-8{_}2351{_}Where the Val d'Assa turned west, our front line ran on one side of the shallow gulley and the Austrian on the other.
10107-8{_}2352{_}The Austrian front line was completely in the open.
10107-8{_}2353{_}The first houses of Asiago were only a few hundred yards behind it.
10107-8{_}2354{_}From the defensive point of view our line was very strong, and the trenches, particularly at the eastern end, very good, deeply blasted in the rock.
10107-8{_}2355{_}The wooded ridge, running close behind our front line all the way, completely hid from the enemy all movement in our rear.
10107-8{_}2356{_}He could get no observation here except by aircraft.
10107-8{_}2357{_}Even movements in our front line, owing to the trees, were largely invisible at a distance, and, owing to the lie of the ground, large parts of No Man's Land could be seen from our own trenches, but from nowhere in the enemy's lines, with the result that we were able to post machine guns, trench mortars and even, for a short time, a field battery there, without being detected, until these weapons had served their immediate purpose.
10107-8{_}2358{_}Our systems of transport, supply and reliefs of the troops in the line could, therefore, be carried out at any hour of the day or night with almost complete disregard of the enemy.
10107-8{_}2359{_}His intermittent shelling of the roads was perfectly blind and haphazard and seldom did us any damage.
10107-8{_}2360{_}He, on the other hand, was in a very undesirable situation.
10107-8{_}2361{_}Not only was his front line all the way in full view from our various ground O.P.
10107-8{_}2362{_}'s, but a long stretch of flat country several miles broad behind his front line was equally in view.
10107-8{_}2363{_}Only a few small folds in the ground were invisible from all points along our ridge.
10107-8{_}2364{_}We could see also most of the nearer slopes of the northern ridge, though here the thick woods and breaks in the hillside gave him greater opportunities for concealment.
10107-8{_}2365{_}Taking into account, therefore, ground observation only, we had him at a tremendous disadvantage.
10107-8{_}2366{_}He dared not move nor show himself in daylight behind his line, and was compelled to carry out all his supply and troop movements at night, or during fogs that might lift at any moment.
10107-8{_}2367{_}One French Battery did no other work except sweep up and down his roads throughout the hours of darkness, and it is obvious that the probable damage done in this way was far greater than anything he could hope to do to us.
10107-8{_}2368{_}Taking into account the possibilities of observation from the air, the balance in our favour became even greater.
10107-8{_}2369{_}We had a strong superiority in the air, whenever it was worth our while to enforce it, partly because our airmen were individually superior to the Austrians, and partly because we had more and better machines.
10107-8{_}2370{_}Our pilots often flew over the northern ridge, both to observe and to bomb, but the enemy seldom crossed the southern ridge.
10107-8{_}2371{_}His anti-aircraft Batteries were, however, at least as good as ours, and, in my opinion, better.
10107-8{_}2372{_}Most of our pre-arranged counter-battery shoots were carried out with aeroplane observation against enemy Batteries situated in the thick woods on the slopes of the northern ridge, the airman flying backwards and forwards over the target and sending us his observations by wireless.
10107-8{_}2373{_}But it was often necessary to spend more than half of the four hundred rounds allotted to a normal counter-battery shoot in destroying the trees round the target, before the airman could get a good view of it.
10107-8{_}2374{_}Flying, however, was always difficult on the Plateau, especially during the winter, and more difficult for our men than for theirs, since there were no feasible landing-places behind our lines.
10107-8{_}2375{_}Our nearest aerodromes were down on the plain, and a big expenditure of petrol was required to get the airman up the mountains and actually over the Plateau, and also to get him down again.
10107-8{_}2376{_}The time during which he could keep in the air for observation was, therefore, very limited.
10107-8{_}2377{_}Weather conditions on the Plateau, moreover, were often very unfavourable for flying even in the spring and summer.
10107-8{_}2378{_}The practical importance of our superiority in the air was thus smaller than might have been expected.
10107-8{_}2379{_}From the defensive point of view, then, our position was pretty strong.
10107-8{_}2380{_}But the sector was important and might at any time become critical, and much depended upon its successful defence.
10107-8{_}2381{_}For the mountain wall that guarded the Italian plain had been worn very thin in this neighbourhood by the Austrian successes of last year.
10107-8{_}2382{_}An Austrian advance of another few miles would bring the enemy over the edge of the mountains, with the plain beneath in full view.
10107-8{_}2383{_}Further defence would then become extremely difficult and costly, and the whole situation, as regards relative superiority of positions and observation, now so greatly in our favour, would be more than reversed.
10107-8{_}2384{_}We were too near the edge to have any elbow room or freedom of manoeuvre.
10107-8{_}2385{_}Our present positions were almost the last that we could hope to hold without very grave embarrassment.
10107-8{_}2386{_}It would have seemed evident, then, that to obtain more elbow room and security, we should not be content with a defensive policy, but should aim at gaining ground and thickening the mountain wall by means of an early local offensive, even if larger operations were not yet practicable.
10107-8{_}2387{_}But, from the offensive point of view, our position presented great difficulties.
10107-8{_}2388{_}To make only a small advance would leave us worse off than now.
10107-8{_}2389{_}Merely to go out into the middle of the Plateau, merely to reoccupy the ruins of Asiago, would be futile, except for a very slight and transitory "moral effect."
10107-8{_}2390{_}To carry the whole Plateau and establish a line along the lower slopes of the northern ridge would be no better.
10107-8{_}2391{_}We should only be taking over the difficulties of the enemy in respect of his exposed positions, while he would escape from these difficulties and obtain an immunity from observation nearly as great as that which we now possessed.
10107-8{_}2392{_}No offensive would benefit us which did not give us, at the very least, the whole of the crest of the northern ridge.
10107-8{_}2393{_}And to aim at this would be a big and risky undertaking, involving perhaps heavy casualties and large reserves.
10107-8{_}2394{_}We had only three British Divisions in Italy at this time, the 7th, 23rd and 48th, two of which were always in the line and one in reserve.
10107-8{_}2395{_}The French had now only two Divisions in Italy and the Italians, when the German advance in France became serious, had sent to France more men than there were French and British left in Italy.
10107-8{_}2396{_}The large fact remained that, since the military collapse of Russia the previous year, the Austrians had brought practically their whole Army on to the Italian Front and established a large superiority over the Italians, both in numbers and in guns.
10107-8{_}2397{_}Considerable Italian reserves had to be kept mobile and ready to meet an Austrian offensive anywhere along the mountain front or on the plain.
10107-8{_}2398{_}There was not likely to be much that could be safely spared to back up a Franco-British offensive on the Plateau.
10107-8{_}2399{_}None the less, the value of a successful offensive here was recognised to be so great, that it was several times on the point of being attempted in the months that followed.
10107-8{_}2400{_}But it did not finally come, until events elsewhere had prepared the way and sapped the enemy's power of resistance.
10107-8{_}2401{_}This, however, is anticipating history.
10107-8{_}2402{_}In March, when we first arrived, we moved into a Battery position in the pine woods behind the rear slope of the southern ridge.
10107-8{_}2403{_}Our right hand gun was only a hundred yards from the cross-roads at Pria dell' Acqua, disagreeably close, as we afterwards discovered.
10107-8{_}2404{_}For the enemy had those cross-roads "absolutely taped," as the expression went.
10107-8{_}2405{_}In other respects the Battery position was a good one.
10107-8{_}2406{_}Being an old Italian position, it had gun pits already blasted in the rock, though they were not quite suited to our guns and line of fire, and we had to do some more blasting for ourselves.
10107-8{_}2407{_}In the course of this, a premature explosion occurred, wounding one of our gunners so severely that he lost one leg and the sight of both his eyes and a few days later, perhaps fortunately, died of other injuries.
10107-8{_}2408{_}He was a Cornishman, very young and very popular with every one in the Battery.
10107-8{_}2409{_}We missed him greatly.
10107-8{_}2410{_}In this same accident Winterton was also injured, and nearly lost an eye.
10107-8{_}2411{_}He went to Hospital and thence to England, and saw no more of the war, for the sight of his eye came back to him but slowly.
10107-8{_}2412{_}The Italians had also blasted some good _caverne_ in the position, and these we gradually enlarged and multiplied, till we had cover for the whole Battery.
10107-8{_}2413{_}Being on the side of a hill, and our guns not constructed to fire at a greater elevation than forty-five degrees (the Italians had fired at "super-elevations" up to eighty), we had to cut down many trees in front of the guns.
10107-8{_}2414{_}But this clearance hardly showed in aeroplane photographs, as there were already many bare patches in the woods.
10107-8{_}2415{_}We had perfect flash-cover behind the ridge and were, indeed, quite invisible, when the guns were camouflaged, even to an aeroplane flying low and immediately overhead.
10107-8{_}2416{_}From our position we could shoot, if necessary, right over the top of the northern ridge, on the other side of the Plateau.
10107-8{_}2417{_}And this was good enough for most purposes.
10107-8{_}2418{_}We prepared another position, which was known as the "Forward" or "Battle Position," at San Sisto, about four hundred yards behind the front line.
10107-8{_}2419{_}This position we never occupied, but we should have done so, if an offensive had come from our side while we were still on the Plateau.
10107-8{_}2420{_}San Sisto, I was told, was once the centre of a leper reservation.
10107-8{_}2421{_}There is a little chapel there, but no other buildings.
10107-8{_}2422{_}This chapel was used by the R.A.M.C.
10107-8{_}2423{_}as a First Aid Post.
10107-8{_}2424{_}One day I saw a shell go clean through the roof of it, but there was no one inside at the time.
10107-8{_}2425{_}The Battery O.P.
10107-8{_}2426{_}was a glorious place, up a tall pine tree on the summit of Cima del Taglio, a high point to the east of the Granezza--Pria dell' Acqua road.
10107-8{_}2427{_}This O.P.
10107-8{_}2428{_}had been built by the French.
10107-8{_}2429{_}It was reached by a strong pinewood ladder, with a small platform half way up as a resting-place.
10107-8{_}2430{_}The O.P.
10107-8{_}2431{_}itself consisted of a wooden platform, nailed to cross pieces, supported on two trees.
10107-8{_}2432{_}It was about fifteen feet long and four feet broad and some ninety feet above the ground.
10107-8{_}2433{_}At one end of the platform a hut had been erected, with a long glass window, opening outward, on the northern side, and a small fixed glass window on the western.
10107-8{_}2434{_}The other end of the platform was uncovered.
10107-8{_}2435{_}When the weather was bad one could shelter in the hut and imagine oneself out at sea, as the trees swayed in the wind.
10107-8{_}2436{_}The O.P.
10107-8{_}2437{_}was well hidden from the enemy by the branches of the trees.
10107-8{_}2438{_}The view was superb.
10107-8{_}2439{_}Immediately below the thick pine forest sloped gradually downwards, the trees still carrying a heavy weight of snow.
10107-8{_}2440{_}Among the trees patches of deep snow were visible, hiding rocky ground.
10107-8{_}2441{_}Beyond lay the Plateau, studded with villages and isolated houses, with the ruins of Asiago in the centre of the view, and, to the left of it, the light railway line and its raised embankment, along which the Austrian trenches ran.
10107-8{_}2442{_}And beyond, more pinewoods on the northern ridge, and beyond, more mountains, one snowy range behind another, up to the horizon.
10107-8{_}2443{_}The visibility was often poor and variable from one minute to another.
10107-8{_}2444{_}Great clouds used to sweep low over the Plateau, blotting out everything but the nearest trees, and then sweep past, and Asiago would come into sudden view again, and the sun would shine forth once more upon the little clusters of white houses, some utterly wrecked, some mere shells, others as yet hardly touched by the destruction of war.
10107-8{_}2445{_}The prosaic name of this O.P.
10107-8{_}2446{_}was "Claud."
10107-8{_}2447{_}There was another O.P.
10107-8{_}2448{_}called Ascot, which we used sometimes to man at the beginning.
10107-8{_}2449{_}It was on, or rather in, Monte Kaberlaba, just behind the front line, approached through a communication trench and then a long tunnel through the rock, named by our troops the Severn Tunnel.
10107-8{_}2450{_}This tunnel was full of water and many worse things, and it was impossible to clean it out properly.
10107-8{_}2451{_}The unfortunate telephonists off duty had to live and sleep in it.
10107-8{_}2452{_}The O.P.
10107-8{_}2453{_}was a cramped, little, stinking place at the far end of the tunnel, shared with the Italians, undoubtedly visible and well known to the enemy, and with practically no view.
10107-8{_}2454{_}The Major, by his usual skilful diplomacy, soon arranged that we should man Claud permanently, but Ascot never.
10107-8{_}2455{_}My only pleasant recollection of Ascot is that once, about midnight, as we were keeping watch together, a young Italian gunner from the Romagna sang to me.
10107-8{_}2456{_}"'Addio, mia bell', addio!'
10107-8{_}2457{_}Cantava nel partir la giovent√π,     Mentre gl' imboscati si stavano     Divertire, giornale in mano     E la sigaretta.
10107-8{_}2458{_}Per noi l'assalto     Alla baionetta!
10107-8{_}2459{_}Come le mosche noi dobbiam morir,     Mentre gl' imboscati si stanno a divertir.
10107-8{_}2460{_}"[1]  [Footnote 1:     "Good-bye, my darling, good-bye!"
10107-8{_}2461{_}Sang the young men as they went away,    While the imboscati were standing about    To amuse themselves, with a newspaper in their hand    And a cigarette.
10107-8{_}2462{_}For us the bayonet charge!
10107-8{_}2463{_}Like flies we must die.
10107-8{_}2464{_}While the imboscati stand about to amuse themselves.
10107-8{_}2465{_}This is one of many front line versions of a patriotic drawing-room song.
10107-8{_}2466{_}It has an admirable tune.]
10107-8{_}2467{_}He sang me also another longer song, composed by a friend of his, which is not fit for reproduction.
10107-8{_}2468{_}*       *       *       *       *  We experienced great variations of weather on the Plateau.
10107-8{_}2469{_}When we first arrived in March the snow was in full thaw, and every road a sunlit, rushing torrent.
10107-8{_}2470{_}We climbed about at that time in gum boots.
10107-8{_}2471{_}Later it snowed again heavily and often.
10107-8{_}2472{_}Sometimes for several days running we were enveloped in a thick mist, and then suddenly it would clear away.
10107-8{_}2473{_}Once, I remember, it cleared at night, and one saw the full moon rising through the pine trees into an utterly clear, ice-cold sky, and under one's feet the hard snow scrunched and glittered in the moonlight.
10107-8{_}2474{_}British, French and Italian Batteries were all mixed together in this sector.
10107-8{_}2475{_}On our left came first another British Battery, then two French, one in front of the road and one behind it, then another British, then an Italian.
10107-8{_}2476{_}On our right, slightly more forward, the Headquarters of an Italian Heavy Artillery Group, in front of them a British and an Italian Battery, one on each side of the road leading past Kaberlaba to the front line.
10107-8{_}2477{_}To the right of the Italian Headquarters, across the San Sisto road, was a French Battery, with two Italian Batteries in front of it.
10107-8{_}2478{_}To our own right rear was one Italian Battery and two French, and in rear of them, back along the road to Granezza, our own Brigade Headquarters.
10107-8{_}2479{_}This mixture was a good arrangement, stimulating friendly rivalry and facilitating _liaison_ and exchange of ideas.
10107-8{_}2480{_}Our relations were specially cordial with the Italian-Group Headquarters and with one of the French Batteries on our left.
10107-8{_}2481{_}The Italian Major commanding this Group was a Mantuan and he and I became firm friends.
10107-8{_}2482{_}It was in his Mess one night, in reply to the toast of the Allies, that I made my first after-dinner speech in Italian.
10107-8{_}2483{_}I do not claim that it was grammatically perfect, but all that I said was, I think, well understood, and I was in no hesitation for words.
10107-8{_}2484{_}Not till the end of May did Spring really climb the mountains, and the snow finally vanish, and then the days, apart from the facts of war, were perfect, blue sky and sunshine all day long among the warm aromatic pines and the freshness of the mountain air.
10107-8{_}2485{_}Here and there, in clearings in the forest, were patches of thick, rich grass, making a bright contrast to the dull, dark green of the pines, and in the grass arose many-coloured wild flowers.
10107-8{_}2486{_}The Italians have buried their dead up here in little groups among the trees, and not in great graveyards.
10107-8{_}2487{_}There was one such little group on the hillside in the middle of our Battery position, between two of our gunpits.
10107-8{_}2488{_}There was another in the middle of our forward position at San Sisto, and another, where some thirty Bersaglieri and Artillerymen were buried, in the Baerenthal Valley.
10107-8{_}2489{_}It was here one day that an Irish Major, newly come to Italy, said to me, "I don't want any better grave than that."
10107-8{_}2490{_}Nor did I.
10107-8{_}2491{_}It was a place of marvellous and eternal beauty, ever changing with the seasons.
10107-8{_}2492{_}It made one's heart ache to be in the midst of it.
10107-8{_}2493{_}It was hither that they brought in the months that followed many of the British dead, who fell in this sector, and laid them beside the Italians, at whose graves we had looked that day.
10107-8{_}2494{_}CHAPTER XXX  SOME NOTES ON NATIONAL CHARACTERISTICS  For a week or two in May an Italian Engineer officer messed with us.
10107-8{_}2495{_}He had a sleeping hut on the hill just behind us, and was in charge of a party of men who were working on British Field Artillery positions.
10107-8{_}2496{_}His men were on British rations and did not altogether like them.
10107-8{_}2497{_}They would have preferred more bread and less meat and jam, and they missed their coffee.
10107-8{_}2498{_}Our tea they did not fancy.
10107-8{_}2499{_}The first time it was issued to them, they thought it was medicine.
10107-8{_}2500{_}"Why do the English give us _'camomila'_?"
10107-8{_}2501{_}they asked their officer, "we are not ill!"
10107-8{_}2502{_}*       *       *       *       *  I have had, at one time and another, much gay and delightful intercourse both with Frenchmen and Italians, which has led me to certain speculative comparisons and to many dangerous generalisations, some of which I will venture tentatively to set down here.
10107-8{_}2503{_}But it is difficult to find forms of words which are not mere journalism.
10107-8{_}2504{_}Italian humour is more primitive and uproarious than French, and the Italians seem to present fewer barriers to intimacy, but the proportion of rational discussion is larger in the conversation of the French.
10107-8{_}2505{_}Both the French and the Italians combine natural and easy good manners with great punctiliousness in small matters of etiquette.
10107-8{_}2506{_}Only very arrogant or very boorish people find it difficult to get on well with either.
10107-8{_}2507{_}It is idle for any wideawake observer to deny that a certain antipathy exists between the French and the Italians.
10107-8{_}2508{_}Both, I think, generally prefer the British to their Latin brothers, and I have heard both say unjust and absurdly untrue things about the other.
10107-8{_}2509{_}Their antipathy is rooted partly in temperament, partly in history, and partly in that ignorance and lack of understanding which accounts for nine-tenths of all international antipathies.
10107-8{_}2510{_}As Charles Lamb said, in an anecdote which President Wilson is fond of quoting, "I cannot hate a man I know."
10107-8{_}2511{_}It is sometimes said that the French and the Italians are too much alike to be in perfect sympathy.
10107-8{_}2512{_}The Frenchman has at times an instinct to be what an Englishman would call "theatrical," which instinct the Englishman himself hardly possesses at all.
10107-8{_}2513{_}But in the Italian this instinct is even stronger than in the Frenchman, and he gives it freer play.
10107-8{_}2514{_}Thus the Frenchman often notices the Italian doing and saying things which he himself dislikes, but which it needs a deliberate effort of self-repression on his part not to imitate.
10107-8{_}2515{_}The Englishman has no inclination to do and say such things, and is, therefore, more tolerant of them than the Frenchman, thinking them either charming or merely "queer," according to his temperament.
10107-8{_}2516{_}If the French are the more admirable, the Italians are the more lovable; if the French are the more creative, the Italians are the more receptive.
10107-8{_}2517{_}In the French, though not so much in the Italians, one does find that "sheer brutality of the Latin intellect," which, since the French Revolution, has dethroned many previously dominant ideas and institutions.
10107-8{_}2518{_}One finds in the French a tradition of limpid precision, of concise and ordered logic, while the Italians are still groping rather turgidly among those great abstract ideas which the French handle so easily.
10107-8{_}2519{_}The spirit of France shines with the hard splendour of the noonday sun, of Italy with the soft radiance of the light of early mornings and late afternoons.
10107-8{_}2520{_}The French are proud and sometimes intolerant, the Italians tolerant and often diffident.
10107-8{_}2521{_}It has been truly said that in every modern Frenchman there is still something Napoleonic, however subconscious it may have become.
10107-8{_}2522{_}One could never be surprised if, in the midst of conversation, a Frenchman should suddenly draw himself up and cry "Vive la France, monsieur!"
10107-8{_}2523{_}But one does not expect an Italian in like circumstances to cry "Viva l'Italia!"
10107-8{_}2524{_}In general, the French are the more tenacious and clear-visioned in adversity, but none are more irresistible in success, nor more conscious of its drama, than the Italians.
10107-8{_}2525{_}The low birth-rate of France, as compared with Italy, is a fact of deep and permanent importance.
10107-8{_}2526{_}In years to come the French will grow more and more negligible, numerically, in world politics, but the French spirit is immortal and unconquerable.
10107-8{_}2527{_}It will penetrate the hearts of the best men for ever, and ideas characteristically and originally French will continue to mould the world's thought and action till the end of time.
10107-8{_}2528{_}The Italians on the other hand will play in future history a greater part numerically, and moreover, by a greater intermarriage with other races, will continue to produce fine and generous human types, not wholly Italian.
10107-8{_}2529{_}Italians will continue to show a shining example to the world by reason of their gaiety and charm of character, their mental subtlety, which with time will grow less involved and more lucid in expression, by their art of life, even now not much inferior to the French, by their sensitiveness to beauty, by their capacity for enthusiastic appreciation, and by their technical genius in applied science.
10107-8{_}2530{_}Italy is a naturally democratic and peaceable polity, and her present imperfections will diminish rapidly with the increase of her national maturity and stability.
10107-8{_}2531{_}She will be a sane and healthy element in the future international order.
10107-8{_}2532{_}In some respects, as in their indifference, sometimes excessive, to foreign opinion, the French resemble the British, just as, in their excessive sensitiveness on this point, the Italians resemble the Americans.
10107-8{_}2533{_}This is the contrast between age and youth, between nations with a continuous tradition of centuries behind them and nations born or reborn only yesterday.
10107-8{_}2534{_}There remains the larger contrast between the Latins on the one hand and the Anglo-Saxons on the other.
10107-8{_}2535{_}At first sight it is the latter who are the more realistic and the more practical, the former who are the more effusive, idealistic and poetical.
10107-8{_}2536{_}But, as Mr Norman Douglas admirably puts it in _South Wind_, "Enclosed within the soft imagination of the _homo Mediterraneus_ lies a kernel of hard reason.
10107-8{_}2537{_}The Northerner's hardness is on the surface; his core, his inner being, is apt to quaver in a state of fluid irresponsibility."
10107-8{_}2538{_}The comparative method of approach to the institution of marriage among Latins and among Anglo-Saxons illustrates this truth.
10107-8{_}2539{_}And it serves also, perhaps, for an example that, in the midst of the terrors of war, the dim project of a League of Nations, the only hope of the world, first took shape in the minds of Anglo-Saxon dreamers and not of Latin realists.
10107-8{_}2540{_}The Latin often thinks more clearly, but not always more profoundly, than the Anglo-Saxon.
10107-8{_}2541{_}The currents on the surface are not always the same as the currents in the deep.
10107-8{_}2542{_}CHAPTER XXXI  ROME IN THE SPRING  I was at Rome in May.
10107-8{_}2543{_}Of the many things and persons I saw there, not much is relevant here.
10107-8{_}2544{_}But there is an intoxication and a beauty and a sense of wonder in Rome in the Spring, as great as I have found at any time elsewhere.
10107-8{_}2545{_}Rome grew upon me, rapidly and ceaselessly, during the few days that I spent there, and sent me back to the mountains, clothed with their pinewoods and their graves of much brave youth, uplifted in heart and purified in spirit.
10107-8{_}2546{_}*       *       *       *       *  Early one afternoon in the Piazza Venezia I fell in with two Italian officers, an Alpino and an Engineer, both wounded and not yet fit to go back to the Front.
10107-8{_}2547{_}We rapidly made friends, and, having drunk beer together, we took a carrozza and drove to the Villa Borghese Gardens, where we walked and sat for several hours.
10107-8{_}2548{_}Then we went back to the Piazza Venezia, and walked in the neighbourhood and contemplated the monuments.
10107-8{_}2549{_}My friends said that Rome was the capital city of the world, and praised also the giant memorial to Italian Unity and Victor Emmanuel II., which, still unfinished, dominates the Piazza, and indeed a large part of the city.
10107-8{_}2550{_}This memorial is, I believe, condemned by the greater part of foreign aesthetic opinion, the Germans alone conspicuously dissenting.
10107-8{_}2551{_}Personally I like it in the fading light from close at hand, and in a bright light from a distance, as one sees it, for instance, from the Pincio.
10107-8{_}2552{_}We spoke a little, but not much, of the war.
10107-8{_}2553{_}They were both for fighting on till final victory, whatever the cost, and both spoke with admiration of the inflexible and stubborn spirit of the British nation.
10107-8{_}2554{_}Very wonderful too is the spirit which animates the Alpini.
10107-8{_}2555{_}My Alpino friend had been wounded in the leg last August at Rombon, and still walked lame.
10107-8{_}2556{_}He told me of incidents which he had witnessed, of Alpini charging across and through uncut enemy wire, with the wounded and the dying crying to their comrades, "Ciao!
10107-8{_}2557{_}[1] Ciao!
10107-8{_}2558{_}Avanti!"
10107-8{_}2559{_}He sang me also certain songs of the Alpini, in one of which they sing that in the Italian tricolour the green stands for the Alpini,[2] the white for the snow on their mountains and the red for their blood.
10107-8{_}2560{_}O these "fiamme verdi," who can talk and sing themselves into such transfigured ecstasies, as to turn, death and pain almost into easy glories!
10107-8{_}2561{_}[Footnote 1: "Ciao" is a colloquialism, much the same as our own "so long," or "good-bye and good luck!"
10107-8{_}2562{_}It is an intimate word, used only between friends at parting.]
10107-8{_}2563{_}[Footnote 2: The regimental colours of the Alpini are plain green, worn on the collar.]
10107-8{_}2564{_}The three of us dined at a little restaurant near the Pantheon, and my friends wrote their names and a greeting to my wife on a post card, and an old man at the next table ordered a bottle of wine, in which we all drank the health of the Allies, and a party at another table began to sing, and went on singing for nearly an hour.
10107-8{_}2565{_}We stayed in that restaurant talking till eleven p.m., when the lights were turned out, and then my friends demanded that we should make another "giro artistico," which terminated beneath Trajan's Column, where in the warm air we sat and talked for half an hour more, and separated about midnight, I having had eight hours of continuous practice in the use of the second person singular of Italian verbs.
10107-8{_}2566{_}*       *       *       *       *  Next day I lunched with my friends the Marinis, at their charming Villa on Monte Parioli, and in the afternoon Signor Marini offered to act as my guide to places of interest.
10107-8{_}2567{_}We took the tram to the Piazza del Popolo, which was laid out in 1810 under the French Empire, perfectly circular and symmetrical, thus differing from the more Italian of Roman Piazzas, such as the elongated and quite unsymmetrical Piazza di Spagna.
10107-8{_}2568{_}We passed along the broad embankment beside the Tiber and through the Square of St Peter's.
10107-8{_}2569{_}Just outside the gates of the Vatican, my guide pointed out to me the little shabby building occupied by the Giordano Bruno Society, symbolic of the brave defiance thrown out, all down the ages, by poverty and the spirit of freedom and intellectual honesty, in the face of wealth and power and oppression, intellectual bondage and the dead weight of tradition.
10107-8{_}2570{_}My guide thought that, out of the wreck of her material defeat and disaster, Russia would perhaps give a new spiritual religion to the western world, to take the place of old forms now dead, and historic organisations which, having lacked the audacity and the wisdom to remain poor when riches were within easy reach, had now become visibly and irremediably detached from the life of the people.
10107-8{_}2571{_}He did not fear, as some did for France, a clerical revival in Italy after the war.
10107-8{_}2572{_}For the Italian branch of clerical power had shown itself in the hour of Italy's deadly peril to be largely lacking in Italian patriotism, and to have been scheming for the maintenance, if not the expansion, of Austrian dominion, and, perhaps, for the re-establishment by the aid of Austrian and German bayonets, or Turkish, if it had been necessary to solicit them, of the Temporal Power of the Papacy over Italian citizens and Italian soil.
10107-8{_}2573{_}I saw one of the Swiss mercenaries of the Papacy gazing forth a little contemptuously through a door of the Vatican upon the secular outer world.
10107-8{_}2574{_}From St Peter's we drove up the Janiculum, stopping on the way at the convent of S. Onofrio, where Tasso passed the last three weeks of his life and where a Tasso Museum has been accumulated.
10107-8{_}2575{_}Very admirable is the equestrian statue of Garibaldi on the Janiculum, both as sculpture and for its details of intention, such as that sideways turning of his head, looking down hill at the Vatican, as though saying, "Non ti dimentico,"--"I do not forget you, my old enemy."
10107-8{_}2576{_}The view of Rome from this point is magnificent, the best that I have seen, though the view from the Pincio only just falls short of it.
10107-8{_}2577{_}Thence, passing outside the old city walls through the Porta San Pancrazio, we stood on ground made memorable by Garibaldi's defence of the Roman Republic in 1849, and went down, past the.
10107-8{_}2578{_}Pope's monument to the French who died fighting to defend his Temporal Power against the Garibaldini, into the beautiful garden of the Villa Pamfili.
10107-8{_}2579{_}"Attendono il finale risorgimento,"[1] says the Pope's Italian version on the monument.
10107-8{_}2580{_}It is an ironical phrase in view of the history of the next twenty years.
10107-8{_}2581{_}"They did not have long to wait," I said, "a bird in the hand is worth two in the bush."
10107-8{_}2582{_}And my guide said, I thought well, of the French that they are a people of great gifts and of most generous mind, but that their rulers have often showed "un po' di volubilit√†, un po' di fantasia."
10107-8{_}2583{_}[Footnote 1: "They await the final resurrection."
10107-8{_}2584{_}But "risorgimento" to most Italians suggests modern history more than theology.]
10107-8{_}2585{_}We visited last of all the Dep√¥t of the Bersaglieri in Trastevere, where is also the famous Bersagliere Museum.
10107-8{_}2586{_}Here we were received and shown round with great courtesy by the Colonel commanding the Dep√¥t, a handsome man with most sad eyes, but full of great regimental pride in this creation, intimately and characteristically Italian, of General La Marmora.
10107-8{_}2587{_}In the Museum, among much that was trivial, I found much that was interesting and even deeply moving: the relics of Enrico Toti, an artist who, having only one leg, joined the Bersaglieri Ciclisti as a volunteer at the beginning of the war, and rode up mountain tracks on a bicycle with a single pedal, and died, after acts of the greatest heroism and after sustaining for many hours grave wounds, crying with his last breath "Avanti Savoia!
10107-8{_}2588{_}", upon whose dead body and brave departed spirit was conferred the most rare Gold Medal for Valour; photographs of all the Bersaglieri, who since the foundation of the Regiment have won the Gold Medal, some twenty of them, hanging together on one wall, all dead now; the steel helmet of a Bersagliere Major, killed on the Carso, while leading his men; this is all that they found of him, but it has three holes through the front, sufficient proof, said the Colonel, that he was not going backward when he died; a menu card, signed by all the officers of a Bersagliere Battalion, who dined together on the eve of the victorious action of Col Valbella last January, in which they played a worthy part.
10107-8{_}2589{_}The Colonel told me that his own son was killed and is buried beyond the Isonzo, near Cervignano.
10107-8{_}2590{_}It had been suggested to him that he should have the body brought home, but he preferred to leave it where it fell.
10107-8{_}2591{_}"C'√® un' idea che √® morta l√¨," he said, "It is an idea which has died there.
10107-8{_}2592{_}Some day, if I live, I shall make a pilgrimage thither, but the Austrians may, by now, have destroyed the grave."
10107-8{_}2593{_}Outside in the courtyard, where the Colonel took leave of us, I saw many young Bersaglieri, the latest batches of recruits, mere boys.
10107-8{_}2594{_}"They are splendid material," he said, with a military pride, not without a half-regretful tenderness, "one can make anything out of them."
10107-8{_}2595{_}They were, indeed, incomparable human stuff, whether for the purposes of peace or war.
10107-8{_}2596{_}They seemed to have the joy of the spring in their eyes, just as that middle-aged Regular soldier had in his the sadness of autumn.
10107-8{_}2597{_}And amid all the beauty of Rome in the spring, I was haunted by the grim refrain, "Nella primavera si combatte e si muore, o soldato,"--"In the springtide men fight and die, young soldier."
10107-8{_}2598{_}*       *       *       *       *  I went away from Rome strengthened in my previous judgment that the Italians are not a militarist nation.
10107-8{_}2599{_}There was no sign of the militarist, as distinct from the military, spirit at the Bersagliere Dep√¥t.
10107-8{_}2600{_}The relations of the Colonel and Signer Marini illustrated this.
10107-8{_}2601{_}They had never met, nor, I think, heard of one another before.
10107-8{_}2602{_}Yet this little civilian seemed to find it quite natural to march into a military barracks without any preliminary inquiries, to walk upstairs and straight into the Commanding Officer's office and, not finding the Commanding Officer there, to send a message into the Officer's Mess, and, the Commanding Officer having come out, to present his card, without any appearance of servility or undue deference, and to ask to be taken round.
10107-8{_}2603{_}And the Colonel seemed to see nothing odd in these proceedings, but placed himself at once at our disposal and showed us everything and talked without aloofness and without reserve to both of us.
10107-8{_}2604{_}I could not help thinking that things would not have happened quite like this at the Dep√¥t of a crack regiment in most other European capitals.
10107-8{_}2605{_}CHAPTER XXXII  THE FIFTEENTH OF JUNE, 1918  I happened to be the officer on duty in the Battery Command Post on the night of June 14th-15th.
10107-8{_}2606{_}There had been a thick fog for several days and not much firing.
10107-8{_}2607{_}No one expected anything unusual.
10107-8{_}2608{_}The Battery was much below strength owing to the ravages of what the doctors in the mountains called "mountain fever" and the doctors on the plain called influenza.
10107-8{_}2609{_}We had, if I remember rightly, about forty men in Hospital owing to this cause alone.
10107-8{_}2610{_}I myself had a touch of it, but, thinking I could probably count on a quiet night, I refused the offer of a brother officer to take my place, coldly calculating that a few nights later, when it would be my turn to take his duty, I might have more to do.
10107-8{_}2611{_}But my hopes of much sleep were soon dispersed.
10107-8{_}2612{_}Orders came in from Brigade for an elaborate counter-battery shoot with gas shell, in two parts, one between 11 p.m. and midnight, the other between 2 and 3 a.m. We had never fired gas shell from six-inch howitzers before, though we had been warned that we should soon be required to do so.
10107-8{_}2613{_}We had no gas shell in the Battery, but we were informed by Brigade that a sufficient quantity would arrive by the time the shoot was to begin.
10107-8{_}2614{_}In fact, however, the first consignment of gas shell was not delivered in time to enable us to take part in the first part of the bombardment, and I was told not to fire high explosive instead, as that would tend to disperse the gas which other Batteries would be simultaneously firing on the same targets.
10107-8{_}2615{_}The method adopted on this and later occasions, when gas was used, was that a number of our own Batteries should concentrate for, say, five minutes at the fastest rate of fire possible on a particular enemy Battery, then all switch together to another enemy Battery, and so on, all coming back together on to the first enemy Battery after an interval sufficient to lull the human elements forming part of the target into a delusive sense of security and a return to slumber without their masks, or, alternatively, to make them wear their masks continously for prolonged hours of expectation, thus subjecting them to much discomfort, depriving them of sleep, lowering their morale, and making them likelier victims for fresh forms of devilment in the morning.
10107-8{_}2616{_}War is a filthy thing, and must be stamped out ruthlessly.
10107-8{_}2617{_}The facts of gas will have helped to drive this simple conviction into many a thick, egotistical, unsensitive head.
10107-8{_}2618{_}But, as has been wisely said, you cannot half make a war of the modern sort, you cannot let a faint savour of regret hang about all your actions, and enervate your will.
10107-8{_}2619{_}And, in plain, brutal truth, our employment of gas was a big factor in determining and hastening the end.
10107-8{_}2620{_}Of the military efficiency of our gas tactics we had much evidence later on.
10107-8{_}2621{_}We joined in the second part of the gas bombardment in the early hours of the 15th of June, and, when this was nearly over, I got orders to fire at my leisure ten rounds of high explosive at "Archibald," which was our code name for a certain Austrian searchlight, which used to sweep round the country from the summit of Monte Mosciagh on the far side of the Plateau.
10107-8{_}2622{_}So I fired the ten rounds, and the officer at one of the O.P.
10107-8{_}2623{_}'s, whom I had previously warned of my intention, reported that Archibald had gone out after the fourth round, and that, judged by the flashes of their explosions, all the rounds had seemed pretty near.
10107-8{_}2624{_}It was now nearly half-past three, and, conscious that I had a high and rising temperature, I determined to lie down and get a few hours' sleep.
10107-8{_}2625{_}Some of the gas shell which had been intended for the first part of the bombardment, but had arrived about four hours too late, was still being unloaded from lorries on the road outside.
10107-8{_}2626{_}But I asked a Corporal to look after this, and send the unloading party to bed as soon as they had finished.
10107-8{_}2627{_}I had just fallen asleep when the Corporal awakened me.
10107-8{_}2628{_}Were the men, he asked, to go on unloading the shell?
10107-8{_}2629{_}Still half asleep, I asked why not?
10107-8{_}2630{_}He said that the road was being shelled.
10107-8{_}2631{_}I pulled myself together and went to the door of the Command Post.
10107-8{_}2632{_}Not only the road, but the whole Battery position and apparently the whole area for some distance round, was being bombarded very violently.
10107-8{_}2633{_}So I ordered every one to take cover.
10107-8{_}2634{_}It was just 3.45 a.m.
10107-8{_}2635{_}I thought for a moment that this was merely Austrian retaliation for our first use of gas and for the shots at Archibald.
10107-8{_}2636{_}In fact, it was the beginning of the big Austrian offensive, which had long been prearranged.
10107-8{_}2637{_}During the last few days the Austrians had brought up a large number of new guns to our sector, and had placed a number of them right out in the open.
10107-8{_}2638{_}And owing to the thick fog our airmen had been able to see nothing.
10107-8{_}2639{_}The bombardment continued with great fury for several hours, with guns of all calibres, but fortunately mostly small, with shrapnel, high explosive, and gas, chiefly lacrimatory, but mixed with a certain quantity of lethal.
10107-8{_}2640{_}Luckily we had pretty good cover, mainly _caverne_ blasted in the rock.
10107-8{_}2641{_}The Command Post itself was proof against anything less than a direct hit from a pretty heavy shell.
10107-8{_}2642{_}It was also supposed to be gas proof, but was not.
10107-8{_}2643{_}I collected about half a dozen men in it who had nowhere else to go, including two A.S.C.
10107-8{_}2644{_}lorry drivers.
10107-8{_}2645{_}Early on, a young Bombardier was hit rather badly in the leg just outside.
10107-8{_}2646{_}We brought him into the Command Post, bandaged his wound and laid him on the camp bed, on which I had been hoping to get some sleep, and there left him till the shelling should abate and it should be reasonably safe to carry him to the dressing-station a quarter of a mile away.
10107-8{_}2647{_}He lay there, I remember, looking like a little tired cherub, and another Bombardier sat beside him and tried to persuade him to go to sleep.
10107-8{_}2648{_}They were very great friends, those two boys, both signallers, and inseparable both on and off duty.
10107-8{_}2649{_}The one who was not wounded went out that same morning and spent hours repairing telephone lines under very heavy fire, for which act he won the Military Medal.
10107-8{_}2650{_}The other, months later, when his wound was healed and he had returned to the Battery, also won the Military Medal for gallantry on the Piave.
10107-8{_}2651{_}The conduct of the two lorry drivers afforded a strong contrast in psychology.
10107-8{_}2652{_}One, a man of middle age, was superbly cheerful.
10107-8{_}2653{_}"They can't keep this up much longer," he said several times with a placid smile, "they haven't the stuff to do it."
10107-8{_}2654{_}The other, though younger, was a bunch of visible nerves.
10107-8{_}2655{_}A shell exploded just behind the Command Post and violently shook the whole structure and a storm of stones hit the log framework.
10107-8{_}2656{_}He collapsed on the floor, and was convinced for a couple of minutes that he had been hit, and for some time after that he was suffering from shell shock.
10107-8{_}2657{_}Such illusions come easily at such times.
10107-8{_}2658{_}A gas shell made a direct hit on one of our smaller dug-outs.
10107-8{_}2659{_}A Sergeant inside was badly gassed.
10107-8{_}2660{_}They put him for the moment in a gas-proof shelter, higher up the hill, and several hours later I saw him being carried away on a stretcher, apparently lifeless.
10107-8{_}2661{_}But he finally pulled through.
10107-8{_}2662{_}A gunner who was with him in the dug-out came running into the Command Post crying out that he also was gassed.
10107-8{_}2663{_}I made him lie flat on the floor, and told him to keep as quiet as he could.
10107-8{_}2664{_}And then I watched his breathing.
10107-8{_}2665{_}It was clear after a minute or two that, if he had had a breath of gas at all, it was only of the slightest.
10107-8{_}2666{_}But, when I told him this, he was very unwilling to believe me.
10107-8{_}2667{_}Another man was hit just outside, and lay on the ground screaming like an animal in pain.
10107-8{_}2668{_}Him, too, we carried into the Command Post, and, later, on a stretcher to the dressing station.
10107-8{_}2669{_}Meanwhile all the telephone lines had gone owing to the shelling, cutting us off from Brigade, other Batteries and O.P.'s.
10107-8{_}2670{_}But intermittent communication was maintained by runners, and signallers were out, hour after hour, mending breaks in the line and showing their invariable gallantry.
10107-8{_}2671{_}Till about six o'clock our orders were to lie low, to keep under cover and not to open fire.
10107-8{_}2672{_}The rain of shells continued without slackening.
10107-8{_}2673{_}We were wonderfully lucky to get off as lightly as we did.
10107-8{_}2674{_}It is one of the most extraordinary phenomena of war, how many shells can fall in a position of no great size, and yet do very little damage.
10107-8{_}2675{_}It was estimated, and I think quite soberly, that at least two thousand rounds were pumped into our Battery position that morning.
10107-8{_}2676{_}It was soon after six that we got orders, passed along from the next Battery up the road, to open fire on our "counter-preparation target."
10107-8{_}2677{_}This was a sign that the advance of the Austrian Infantry had either begun, or was thought to be imminent.
10107-8{_}2678{_}They attacked, in fact, about a quarter to seven on our sector.
10107-8{_}2679{_}Their synchronising was faulty, as between the different sectors attacked.
10107-8{_}2680{_}Some went forward earlier and others later than had been intended.
10107-8{_}2681{_}They were all newly equipped and were carrying full packs and blankets on their backs.
10107-8{_}2682{_}They had been told by their officers that this was to be the last great offensive of the war, that they were going to drive us headlong down the mountain side, that after two days they would be in Verona, and after ten days in Rome.
10107-8{_}2683{_}They were not told that they had British troops in front of them.
10107-8{_}2684{_}They came forward bravely and with great determination, in five successive waves.
10107-8{_}2685{_}On the British left Divisional Front, to the west of us, they gained a large initial success, and pushed us back well behind our first line of guns.
10107-8{_}2686{_}Here for some time the situation looked serious.
10107-8{_}2687{_}But next day strong counter-attacks by British and Italian troops restored the line, our lost guns were retaken and the retreating Austrians suffered great slaughter and demoralisation.
10107-8{_}2688{_}On the British right Divisional Front, in support of which our Brigade was operating, the British 23rd Division fought a fight worthy of their high reputation.
10107-8{_}2689{_}Forced back for a while from their front line trenches, after a prolonged and intense bombardment and by an overwhelming superiority of numbers, they never even fell back to their support line.
10107-8{_}2690{_}But, turning on the enemy who was advancing along and astride the San Sisto road, they drove him back and re-established their own front line within six hours of the first attack.
10107-8{_}2691{_}It was here that a boy Colonel, a Sherwood Forester scarcely twenty-one years old, won the V.C.
10107-8{_}2692{_}and fell severely wounded.
10107-8{_}2693{_}When things looked black, he had organised the defence and the subsequent counter-attack, collecting together British Infantrymen of several Battalions, together with British Artillerymen and Italian Machine-Gunners and Engineers, welding them into a coherent force and making swift, yet well thought out, dispositions which did much to save the situation.
10107-8{_}2694{_}On the right of the British, the French Infantry, though furiously assaulted, never, I believe, budged an inch.
10107-8{_}2695{_}On the right of the French, the Italians were momentarily driven from Col Valbella, Col del Rosso and Col d'Echele, which they had won in January, but retook all three a few days later.
10107-8{_}2696{_}But we in the Battery knew nothing of all this at the time.
10107-8{_}2697{_}We knew only that we had to open fire on our counter-preparation target.
10107-8{_}2698{_}The gunpit of our No.
10107-8{_}2699{_}1 gun near the cross-roads was in low-lying ground, now so full of gas that one could hardly see one's hand before one's face.
10107-8{_}2700{_}Fortunately we could achieve the rate of fire required by using three guns only, so we left No.
10107-8{_}2701{_}1 out of action for the time.
10107-8{_}2702{_}The enemy's bombardment, as far as we were concerned, was beginning to slacken a little, but was still heavy.
10107-8{_}2703{_}The Major, out on the road with a signaller mending wire, was hit in the face with shrapnel.
10107-8{_}2704{_}It turned out, happily, not a serious wound, but at the time it looked less hopeful.
10107-8{_}2705{_}He went down the mountains in the same Field Ambulance with the young Colonel of the Sherwood Foresters, of whom I have already spoken.
10107-8{_}2706{_}There was an abandoned Field Ambulance in the road, half in the ditch, with the engine still running.
10107-8{_}2707{_}The driver had found the shelling too hot to stay.
10107-8{_}2708{_}There was no one inside it, but we got a couple of stretchers from it.
10107-8{_}2709{_}And we had need of them.
10107-8{_}2710{_}No.
10107-8{_}2711{_}4 gun, my own gun, which was nearest to the road, suffered most severely.
10107-8{_}2712{_}Seven of the detachment on this gun were hit, not all at once but, what is apt to be much more demoralising, at intervals of a few minutes.
10107-8{_}2713{_}A Bombardier was in charge of the gun that day, no senior N.C.O.
10107-8{_}2714{_}'s being available.
10107-8{_}2715{_}He showed a very wonderful coolness and courage.
10107-8{_}2716{_}Shells were bursting all round the gunpit, and sometimes in the gunpit itself.
10107-8{_}2717{_}But the rate of fire never slackened.
10107-8{_}2718{_}Every now and again the cry was heard "another casualty on No.
10107-8{_}2719{_}4!"
10107-8{_}2720{_}and stretcher bearers would start down the road from the Command Post.
10107-8{_}2721{_}But, each time, almost before they had started, came the deep report of another round fired.
10107-8{_}2722{_}No casualties and no shelling could silence her.
10107-8{_}2723{_}At one time this Bombardier had only two other men to help him work the gun.
10107-8{_}2724{_}And both of them were as undismayed as he.
10107-8{_}2725{_}He won the Military Medal for his gallantry that day, and I was very proud of him and of No.
10107-8{_}2726{_}4.
10107-8{_}2727{_}The Brigade Chaplain appeared in the course of the morning and gave a hand in carrying the wounded away on stretchers.
10107-8{_}2728{_}It was outside his official work and I give him all credit and respect for the help he gave us.
10107-8{_}2729{_}But one N.C.O.
10107-8{_}2730{_}in the Battery, with the plain speaking that comes naturally in the face of common danger, said to him, "Well, Sir, we never thought much of you before, either as a man or as a preacher, but we're glad to see you here to-day doing your bit."
10107-8{_}2731{_}The Austrian gunners had a fine sense of discrimination in their targets.
10107-8{_}2732{_}The wooden hut, in which I and two of my brother officers used to sleep, had been hit two or three times that day, and much of our kit had been destroyed.
10107-8{_}2733{_}So had both volumes of Morley's _Rousseau_, which were on a shelf over my bed, leaving behind only a few torn and scattered pages.
10107-8{_}2734{_}Much damage had also been done to a collection of Pompeian photographs of great historical interest.
10107-8{_}2735{_}But Baedeker's _Northern Italy_, which lay alongside, had not been touched!
10107-8{_}2736{_}*       *       *       *       *  The God of Battles also discriminates delicately.
10107-8{_}2737{_}He takes the best and leaves the worst behind.
10107-8{_}2738{_}There died that day, struck by a shell at the foot of our tree O.P.
10107-8{_}2739{_}on Cima del Taglio, one of the finest personalities in the Battery, a signalling Bombardier who had worked for some years on a railway in America and, just before the war, as a railway clerk in the Midlands.
10107-8{_}2740{_}He was the father of a young family, thoughtful and capable, and loyal without subservience to those of higher military rank, in so far as he judged them to be worthy of his loyalty.
10107-8{_}2741{_}I remember one night at the beginning of the year, when we were keeping watch together among the snows at Col d'Astiago, with the sky cold and clear and full of stars, and when he and I talked in complete understanding and agreement of the waste of war and the deeper purposes of life and the need to build up a better world.
10107-8{_}2742{_}Now he is buried in the beautiful Baerenthal Valley, along which runs the road from Pria dell' Acqua to San Sisto and Asiago.
10107-8{_}2743{_}As that day ended, which the Italians always afterwards spoke of as "il giorno quindici" (the fifteenth day), the firing on both sides in our sector slackened, though our guns were seldom silent for more than an hour at a time, and the Austrians still carried out sudden bursts of vicious fire in our neighbourhood.
10107-8{_}2744{_}But that night, and the next day and the next, we began to get through information of what had been happening all along the line.
10107-8{_}2745{_}And when, a week later, the whole tale could be told, it was evident that no great offensive on any Front during this war, prepared with so great elaboration and carried out with so great resources, had ever quite so blankly failed, as the great Austrian offensive from the Astico to the Sea.
10107-8{_}2746{_}And the effect upon the self-confidence and morale of the Italian Army and of the Allied contingents was correspondingly great.
10107-8{_}2747{_}For, to speak frankly, this offensive had been awaited with much apprehension and anxiety, with the memory of Caporetto not yet faded and in view of the success of the German offensive in France.
10107-8{_}2748{_}CHAPTER XXXIII  IN THE TRENTINO  The Austrian offensive on the mountain sector, from the Astico to Monte Grappa, had been obviously and decisively broken by the 18th of June.
10107-8{_}2749{_}But there was still danger on the plain, particularly in the Montello sector, where the Austrians were established in strong force west of the Piave.
10107-8{_}2750{_}A flying Brigade of British Heavy Artillery was hurriedly formed and sent down the mountains.
10107-8{_}2751{_}Of this Brigade my own Battery formed part.
10107-8{_}2752{_}Our general function was to reinforce the Italian Artillery in what was at the moment the most critical sector of the whole Front, our particular function to destroy by shell fire the Piave bridges behind the Austrian troops.
10107-8{_}2753{_}But when we arrived we found that the emergency had already passed.
10107-8{_}2754{_}The bridges had already been destroyed by airmen and Italian Artillery, and the Austrian forces had either been driven back across or into the river by Italian counter-attacks, or had been cut off and compelled to surrender.
10107-8{_}2755{_}We, therefore, came back to the Plateau without firing a round.
10107-8{_}2756{_}But we did not remain there long.
10107-8{_}2757{_}The idea of a mobile Artillery of manoeuvre was much talked of at this time, and early in July a Brigade consisting of three British Siege Batteries, my own included, was moved westwards up into the Trentino.
10107-8{_}2758{_}We travelled all the way by road, through Verona up to Brescia, "the eagle that looks over Lombardy," and thence beside Lake Idro, up the Val Chiese, past Storo into the Val D'Ampola.
10107-8{_}2759{_}All this last stretch of country is famous in Italian history as the scene of Garibaldi's campaign of 1866, which, had it not been interrupted by the course of events elsewhere, would probably have hastened the liberation of Trento by more than half a century, and greatly modified the problems of Italian policy in recent years.
10107-8{_}2760{_}The story is well known of the recall of Garibaldi, which reached him at the moment of victory at Bezzecca, and of his famous reply, a model of laconic self-discipline, in the one word "Ubbidisco"--"I obey."
10107-8{_}2761{_}The little town of Bezzecca lay this July behind the Italian lines, but in full view and easy range of the Austrians.
10107-8{_}2762{_}A company of Arditi was billeted here, with whom I lunched one day, returning from a front line reconnaissance.
10107-8{_}2763{_}The Piazza had been renamed by the Italians "Piazza Ubbidisco," and under cover of darkness they set up one night on the mountain side just above the town a memorial stone to Garibaldi and his volunteers of 1866, a provocative target for Austrian gunners.
10107-8{_}2764{_}No other British troops, except these three Batteries of ours, ever fought in the Trentino.
10107-8{_}2765{_}It was a proud distinction and a very memorable experience.
10107-8{_}2766{_}The natural scenery was superb, a series of great mountain ranges, uneven lines of jagged peaks, enclosing deep cut valleys, the lower slopes of the mountains densely wooded, the higher levels bare precipitous rock.
10107-8{_}2767{_}The Austrian front line ran along one ridge of peaks and ours along another; between ran a deep valley, all No Man's Land, into which patrols used to climb down at night, often with the aid of ropes.
10107-8{_}2768{_}One mountain mass, a continuation of Cima d'Oro, was partly in our possession and partly in theirs, and up there by night among the rocks patrols grappled for the mastery, poised high above the world, and in these struggles men sometimes slipped, or were thrown, to crash to death thousands of feet below in the Val di Ledro.
10107-8{_}2769{_}This country was Austrian before the war, though inhabited wholly by Italians, and Italian troops had conquered it with extraordinary feats of endurance and daring in their first great onrush all along their old frontiers in the spring of 1915.
10107-8{_}2770{_}But now a big advance here by either side, in the face of carefully prepared opposition, seemed almost inconceivable, except as the result of some wide turning movement, hinging on some point many miles away.
10107-8{_}2771{_}The special military problems presented by warfare in such country were numerous and difficult.
10107-8{_}2772{_}Our guns had to be dragged into position up a rough mountain track, which at some points was too narrow and at others too weak to allow the passage of a six-inch howitzer without much preliminary blasting and building up.
10107-8{_}2773{_}Our first gun to go up took twenty-four hours of continuous labour between the time of starting up the track and the time of arriving in position, a distance of only about two miles of zig-zag.
10107-8{_}2774{_}No tractor or other power engine could be used here.
10107-8{_}2775{_}The only force available was that of men hauling on drag ropes, and a party of sixty Italian gunners reinforced our men.
10107-8{_}2776{_}What may be called the problems of pure gunnery were still more difficult.
10107-8{_}2777{_}British Heavy guns had never fired under such conditions before and, for the benefit of such of my readers as may be practical Artillerymen, it may be interesting to remark that for one of our targets the angle of sight, properly so called, worked out at more than twenty degrees, while the map-range elevation was only about fifteen.
10107-8{_}2778{_}The devising of an accurate formula for correction of elevation for a large "_dislivello_," as the Italians shortly call it, which in English means a large "difference of level" between a gun and its target, is one of the most intricate problems of theoretical gunnery, or, for that matter, of theoretical mechanics, involving, among other factors, the various shapes and sizes of projectiles, their comparative steadiness during flight, the resistance of the air, and the effect of other atmospheric conditions and of the force of gravity.
10107-8{_}2779{_}There was a splendid opportunity for systematically testing various rival formulae in the Trentino, but it was allowed to slip.
10107-8{_}2780{_}Among gunners, as among other classes, and especially among Regular Army gunners, the so-called practical man sees little value in scientific experiments, which do not produce large, obvious and quick returns.
10107-8{_}2781{_}We fired many hundred rounds in the Trentino and I have no doubt that they were tolerably effective.
10107-8{_}2782{_}But most of them were fired at night, with no observation possible, and we were often restricted in our registrations by daylight to four rounds a section per target, from which no really reliable conclusions could be drawn.
10107-8{_}2783{_}[1]  [Footnote 1: We could get no help from Italian range tables, which were not merely for different guns and ammunition, but were drawn up on different principles from our own.]
10107-8{_}2784{_}*       *       *       *       *  We were billeted in the village of Tiarno di Sotto, where the Mayor under the Austrian regime, an Italian by race, was still carrying on his duties.
10107-8{_}2785{_}"But I shall have to disappear, if the Austrians ever come back," he said with a smile.
10107-8{_}2786{_}It was a tremendous climb from our billets to get anywhere, the least tremendous being to our Battery position, straight up the nearest mountain side.
10107-8{_}2787{_}A very active and energetic man could get up in a quarter of an hour.
10107-8{_}2788{_}It used to take me twenty minutes.
10107-8{_}2789{_}The weather, moreover, was hot, though considerably cooler than on the plains.
10107-8{_}2790{_}Some Czecho-Slovaks were billeted in the next house to ours, but, owing to lack of a common language, we were unfortunately unable to talk to them.
10107-8{_}2791{_}They were well-built fellows, and gave one an impression of great tenacity and intelligence.
10107-8{_}2792{_}And I know that they were fine fighters.
10107-8{_}2793{_}But they had not the gaiety of the Italians, partly perhaps because they were exiles in a strange land, and must so remain till the day of final victory, which might then have seemed still infinitely remote.
10107-8{_}2794{_}An amusing incident happened one evening.
10107-8{_}2795{_}Four officers had deserted from the Austrian lines and surrendered to the Czecho-Slovaks; it was one of their military functions to induce surrenders.
10107-8{_}2796{_}Two of these officers were themselves Czecho-Slovaks, the third a Jugo-Slav and the fourth an Italian from Istria.
10107-8{_}2797{_}They were very hungry and were in the midst of a good meal, in the presence of a Czecho-Slovak guard, when a Corporal and two gunners from our Battery, passing outside the house and hearing some language being spoken within, which they recognised to be neither English not Italian, rightly thought it their duty to enter and investigate the matter.
10107-8{_}2798{_}The deserters were astonished to see these unfamiliar looking persons, speaking a strange tongue and wearing a uniform which they had never seen before.
10107-8{_}2799{_}But they were still more astonished to learn that they were British.
10107-8{_}2800{_}They seemed hardly to be aware that the British were at war with Austria, much less that any British troops had been within hundreds of miles of them.
10107-8{_}2801{_}The incident closed in much mirth and friendliness.
10107-8{_}2802{_}In the village were also billeted many Italian troops, who used to fill the night with song, long after most of us had gone to bed:--     "'Addio, mia bell', addio!'
10107-8{_}2803{_}Cantava nel partir la giovent√π,"  which is never very far from the lips of any Italian soldier, and those endless _stornelli_, which to an invariable tune they multiply from day to day.
10107-8{_}2804{_}"II General Cadorna     Mangiava la bifstecca;     Ai poveri soldati     Si dava castagna secca,"[1]  [Footnote 1: "General Cadorna used to eat beefsteak.
10107-8{_}2805{_}To the poor soldiers they gave dried chestnuts."]
10107-8{_}2806{_}or     "Il Re dal fronte Giulio     Ha scritto alla Regina,     'Arrivato a Trieste     Ti mander√≤ una cartolina,'"[1]  [Footnote 1: "The King has written to the Queen from the Julian Front 'when I get to Trieste, I will send you a picture post card.'"]
10107-8{_}2807{_}with its sardonic variant or sequel,     "Il General Cadorna     Ha scritto alla Regina     'Se vuoi veder Trieste,     Compra una cartolina.
10107-8{_}2808{_}'"[1]  [Footnote 1: "General Cadorna has written to the Queen, 'if you want to see Trieste, buy a picture post card.'"]
10107-8{_}2809{_}Many of the others are for various reasons unprintable, though many are extremely witty and amusing.
10107-8{_}2810{_}Even those which I have quoted were nominally forbidden by the High Command to be sung, but the prohibition was not very rigorously enforced.
10107-8{_}2811{_}And General Cadorna, after all, had now passed into history.
10107-8{_}2812{_}Of his successor I never heard any evil sung, though I remember once hearing a great crowd of soldiers and civilians at Genoa shouting monotonously.
10107-8{_}2813{_}"Viva, viva il Generale Dia!"
10107-8{_}2814{_}The refrain of the _stornelli_ was onomatopoeic, and was intended to represent the sound of gunfire.
10107-8{_}2815{_}"Bim Bim Bom,     Bim Bim Bom,     Al rombo del cannon."
10107-8{_}2816{_}*       *       *       *       *  What a theatrical country Italy is!
10107-8{_}2817{_}I remember being out in the streets of Tiarno one evening with a stream of song issuing from almost every house, and looking up at the full moon riding high over the towering peaks that locked in our valley and all but shut out the night sky.
10107-8{_}2818{_}I could hardly believe that it was neither a stage setting nor a dream.
10107-8{_}2819{_}I remember another day, when I did a great climb above Bezzecca to carry out a front line reconnaissance, and arrived limp and perspiring to lunch at the Headquarters of an Italian Artillery Group, high, high up, looking out upon a glorious and astounding view.
10107-8{_}2820{_}And in the afternoon I took my first ride on a _teleferica,_ or aerial railway, slung along a steel rope across the deeps, seated on a sort of large wooden tea tray, some six feet long and two and a half across, with a metal rim some six inches high running round the edge.
10107-8{_}2821{_}I was quite prepared to be sick or at least giddy.
10107-8{_}2822{_}But I was pleasantly disappointed.
10107-8{_}2823{_}My journey took about a quarter of an hour; walking it would have taken about three hours of very stiff climbing.
10107-8{_}2824{_}The motion is quite steady, except for a slight jolt as one passes each standard, and, provided one sits still and doesn't shift one's centre of gravity from side to side, there is no wobbling of the tea tray.
10107-8{_}2825{_}And looking down from time to time I saw tree tops far below me, and men and mules on mountain tracks as black specks walking.
10107-8{_}2826{_}*       *       *       *       *  There were various theories to account for our being sent to the Trentino.
10107-8{_}2827{_}One was that an Austrian attack was feared there, another that an Italian attack was intended, but that the intention was afterwards abandoned, a third that the whole thing was a feint to puzzle the Austrians.
10107-8{_}2828{_}But in any case we did not remain there long.
10107-8{_}2829{_}By the beginning of August we were back on the Plateau.
10107-8{_}2830{_}On the return journey, which was again by road all the way, we were given three days' rest at Desenzano and I was able to spend half a day in Verona.
10107-8{_}2831{_}CHAPTER XXXIV  SIRMIONE AND SOLFERINO  "Leave is a privilege and not a right," according to a hack quotation from the King's Regulations.
10107-8{_}2832{_}This quotation has done good service in the mouth of more than one Under Secretary of State for War, heading off tiresome questioners in the British House of Commons.
10107-8{_}2833{_}Leave was a very rare privilege for the British Forces in Italy.
10107-8{_}2834{_}In France, taking a rough average of all ranks and periods, British troops got leave once a year.
10107-8{_}2835{_}In my Battery in Italy, the majority were without leave home for nineteen months.
10107-8{_}2836{_}How much longer they would have had to wait, if the war had not conveniently come to an end in the nineteenth month of their Italian service, I do not know.
10107-8{_}2837{_}Even in Italy, of course, the privilege was extended somewhat more freely to junior regimental officers and much more freely to Staff officers and Lieutenant-Colonels, in view of the danger of brain fag and nervous strain following upon their greater mental exertions and their abnormal exposure to shell fire and the weather.
10107-8{_}2838{_}The former class went home about every eleventh, the latter about every third month.
10107-8{_}2839{_}The French Parliament fairly early in the war, with that gross lack of discrimination and of military understanding habitual to politicians, insisted on the granting of leave every three months to all ranks in all theatres of war.
10107-8{_}2840{_}The Italian Parliament pedantically laid down a uniform period of six months.
10107-8{_}2841{_}The British Parliament, with the sure political instinct of our race, preferred to leave the whole matter in the hands of the War Office.
10107-8{_}2842{_}The interference in purely military affairs of unpractical sentimentalists was strongly discouraged at Westminster.
10107-8{_}2843{_}Why no leave to England could be granted except in special cases, was cogently explained from time to time during the summer in circulars written by Staff officers of high rank, who had frequent opportunities of informing themselves of the realities of the situation, while visiting London.
10107-8{_}2844{_}These circulars were read out on parade and treated with the respect which they deserved.
10107-8{_}2845{_}To allay possible, though quite unreasonable, unrest, it was determined to open a British Club, or Rest Camp, at Sirmione, which, as every reader of Tennyson knows, stands on the tip of a long promontory at the southern end of Lake Garda.
10107-8{_}2846{_}Here a week's holiday was granted to a large proportion of the officers and a small proportion of the rank and file.
10107-8{_}2847{_}Many officers went there more than once.
10107-8{_}2848{_}Two large hotels were hired, which had been chiefly frequented before the war by corpulent and diseased Teutons, for whom a special course of medical treatment, including sulphur baths, used to be prescribed.
10107-8{_}2849{_}One of these hotels was now set apart for British officers, the other for men.
10107-8{_}2850{_}A funny little person in red tabs was put in charge; there were various speculations as to his past activities, but all agreed that he had got into a good job now, and wasn't going to lose it, if tact could prevent it.
10107-8{_}2851{_}This little man used to stand outside the hotel gates as each week's guests arrived from the steamer, and always had a cheery smile of welcome for every Field officer; to General officers he showed special attentions.
10107-8{_}2852{_}He took his meals in the same room as the rest of us, but at what was known as "the Staff table," where he invited to join him any officers of high rank, who might be staying at the hotel, or, if there were none such available, certain of his private friends.
10107-8{_}2853{_}The food supplied to ordinary people like myself was good, wholesome, reasonably plentiful and cheap.
10107-8{_}2854{_}At "the Staff table" special delicacies were provided and additional courses, with no increase of charge.
10107-8{_}2855{_}The profits, he used to say, were made entirely on the drinks and smokes.
10107-8{_}2856{_}A series of rules was drawn up, that none of us might be led into any avoidable temptation.
10107-8{_}2857{_}All towns within reach,--Milan, Verona, Mantua, Brescia, Peschiera,--were placed out of bounds.
10107-8{_}2858{_}So, too, were some of the larger villages on the shores of the Lake.
10107-8{_}2859{_}The hours during which alcoholic liquor might be obtained, either in the Hotels or in the Cafes of Sirmione, were narrowly limited.
10107-8{_}2860{_}Beer was strictly rationed.
10107-8{_}2861{_}Carefully regulated excursions on the Lake, by steamer or launch, were permitted and even encouraged.
10107-8{_}2862{_}Likewise bathing.
10107-8{_}2863{_}I spent a week here, from August 14th to 21st, in gloriously fine, hot weather.
10107-8{_}2864{_}Some said that the damp heat was relaxing and depressing, but I, in my second Italian summer, was getting acclimatised.
10107-8{_}2865{_}The place was wonderfully beautiful.
10107-8{_}2866{_}The end of the promontory is covered with olive trees, the ground thickly carpeted with wild mint and thyme, surrounded on three sides by the deep blue water of the Lake, along the shores of which lie little white villages, backed by groups of straight, dark cypresses, with mountain ranges rising in the background, range behind range, and overhead the hot Italian sun, shining from a cloudless sky.
10107-8{_}2867{_}Here, at the point, were the ruins of what are called, upon what evidence I know not, the Villa, the Baths and the Grotto of Catullus.
10107-8{_}2868{_}Here, too, was an Italian Anti-Aircraft Battery, and the Grotto of Catullus was filled with their ammunition.
10107-8{_}2869{_}The Austrians still held the upper end of the Lake, including the town of Riva.
10107-8{_}2870{_}But only Italian motor boats now survived on the Lake, occasionally raiding Riva.
10107-8{_}2871{_}The Austrian boats had all been sunk early in the war.
10107-8{_}2872{_}*       *       *       *       *  On the 15th I went round the lower end of the Lake in a steamer and, passing along the shores of the beautiful Isola di Garda, on which stands the less beautiful Villa Borghese, landed at Maderno, famous for its lemon groves.
10107-8{_}2873{_}Here a church was being used as a ration store.
10107-8{_}2874{_}It had fine carving on the door.
10107-8{_}2875{_}The French had established Artillery and Machine Gun Schools close to the Lake and several of their officers were on the steamer.
